  const [errors, setErrors] = React.useState({});
  const [touched, setTouched] = React.useState({});
  const [isSubmitting, setIsSubmitting] = React.useState(false);

  const handleChange = React.useCallback((e) =&gt; {
    const { name, value } = e.target;
    setValues(prev =&gt; ({ ...prev, [name]: value }));
  }, []);

  const handleBlur = React.useCallback((e) =&gt; {
    const { name } = e.target;
    setTouched(prev =&gt; ({ ...prev, [name]: true }));
    if (validate) {
      const validationErrors = validate(values);
      setErrors(validationErrors);
    }
  }, [validate, values]);

  const handleSubmit = React.useCallback((e) =&gt; {
    e.preventDefault();
    const validationErrors = validate ? validate(values) : {};
    setErrors(validationErrors);
    setTouched(Object.keys(values).reduce((acc, key) =&gt; ({ ...acc, [key]: true }), {}));
    if (Object.keys(validationErrors).length === 0) {
      setIsSubmitting(true);
      Promise.resolve(onSubmit(values)).finally(() =&gt; setIsSubmitting(false));
    }
  }, [validate, values, onSubmit]);

  return { values, errors, touched, isSubmitting, handleChange, handleBlur, handleSubmit };
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q37">
          <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are React 19's new hooks?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="hooks-q37" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          React 19 introduces several new hooks and changes: (1) <code>use(promise | context)</code> — a new primitive that can read a promise's value during rendering (suspend while pending) or read context; it can be called inside loops and conditions, unlike <code>useContext</code>; (2) <code>useFormStatus()</code> — provides the status of the parent <code>&lt;form&gt;</code> element (<code>pending</code>, <code>data</code>, <code>method</code>, <code>action</code>) — useful for submit buttons that need to know if the form is submitting; (3) <code>useActionState(action, initialState)</code> — manages state returned by a server action or form action function; (4) <code>useOptimistic(state, updateFn)</code> — shows an optimistic update while a mutation is in progress, automatically rolling back when the mutation completes. React 19 also removes the need for <code>forwardRef</code> (refs work as regular props) and stabilizes the React Compiler (auto-memoization).
          <pre class="code-block">// React 19: useOptimistic
function LikeButton({ postId, initialLikeCount }) {
  const [likeCount, setLikeCount] = React.useState(initialLikeCount);
  const [optimisticCount, addOptimisticLike] = React.useOptimistic(
    likeCount,
    (currentCount, increment) =&gt; currentCount + increment
  );

  async function handleLike() {
    addOptimisticLike(1); // immediately show +1
    try {
      const newCount = await likePost(postId);
      setLikeCount(newCount); // update with real server value
    } catch {
      // optimistic update rolls back automatically on error
    }
  }

  return &lt;button onClick={handleLike}&gt;❤️ {optimisticCount}&lt;/button&gt;;
}

// React 19: useFormStatus
function SubmitButton() {
  const { pending } = ReactDOM.useFormStatus();
  return &lt;button type="submit" disabled={pending}&gt;{pending ? 'Saving...' : 'Save'}&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q38">
          <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;A component re-renders on every parent render even with useCallback and useMemo. What could be wrong?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="hooks-q38" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          This is a classic debugging challenge. Possible causes: (1) <code>useCallback</code> has an unstable dependency — one of its deps (an object, another function) is a new reference every render, making the callback itself change every render; (2) <code>useMemo</code> has the same issue — a dep that looks stable but isn't (e.g., the component receives a function prop from above that is not memoized); (3) The child component is consuming a Context that changes on every render (even if the specific props haven't changed, <code>useContext</code> re-renders on any context value change); (4) The <code>React.memo</code> comparator is not properly comparing complex props; (5) The child renders via an index key that changes on reorder. Debugging: use React DevTools "why did this render?" to see which exact prop or hook caused the re-render, then trace where that value originates.
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q39">
          <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How would you manage a complex wizard form with multiple steps using hooks?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="hooks-q39" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          A multi-step wizard form requires: current step tracking, accumulated form data across steps, per-step validation, navigation (next/back), and final submission. The cleanest pattern uses <code>useReducer</code> for the wizard state (step index + accumulated data) because multiple related state pieces change together on each transition. Each step is a separate component receiving the accumulated data and dispatch as props. Validation happens per step in the <code>NEXT</code> action. This pattern is composable, testable (the reducer is a pure function), and scales to many steps.
          <pre class="code-block">const wizardReducer = (state, action) =&gt; {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'NEXT':
      return { ...state, step: state.step + 1, data: { ...state.data, ...action.payload } };
    case 'BACK':
      return { ...state, step: state.step - 1 };
    case 'SUBMIT':
      return { ...state, submitting: true };
    default:
      return state;
  }
};

function Wizard() {
  const [{ step, data, submitting }, dispatch] = React.useReducer(wizardReducer, {
    step: 0, data: {}, submitting: false
  });

  const steps = [PersonalInfoStep, ContactStep, ReviewStep];
  const StepComponent = steps[step];

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;ProgressBar current={step} total={steps.length} /&gt;
      &lt;StepComponent
        data={data}
        onNext={(stepData) =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'NEXT', payload: stepData })}
        onBack={() =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'BACK' })}
        onSubmit={() =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'SUBMIT' })}
      /&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q40">
          <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;You have a component that makes an API call on mount but users are reporting stale data. How do you handle it?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="hooks-q40" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          Stale data from a mount-only fetch (<code>useEffect(..., [])</code>) is a common problem — the component fetches once and never refreshes. Solutions depend on how fresh data needs to be: (1) Add relevant dependencies to the dependency array so the effect re-fetches when those change (e.g., include <code>userId</code> so data refreshes when the user changes); (2) Manual refetch — expose a <code>refetch</code> function that re-runs the fetch (useful for "Refresh" buttons); (3) Polling — re-fetch on an interval with <code>setInterval</code> (appropriate for dashboards); (4) React Query — use <code>staleTime</code> and <code>refetchOnWindowFocus</code> to control freshness; by default React Query refetches on window focus, which handles many "came back to the tab" staleness issues; (5) WebSockets/SSE — for truly real-time data, push updates from the server instead of polling. React Query is the recommended solution as it handles all these cases declaratively.
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q41">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useAsync custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q41" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A <code>useAsync</code> hook abstracts the async state machine pattern: idle &rarr; loading &rarr; success/error. It manages the <code>status</code>, <code>data</code>, and <code>error</code> state and provides an <code>execute</code> function the caller invokes with any arguments. It should handle cancellation via a mounted flag to prevent state updates after unmount, offer a reset capability, and optionally execute immediately on mount when passed <code>immediate = true</code>. The <code>execute</code> callback is stabilized with <code>useCallback</code> so it is safe to use as a dependency in other effects. This pattern replaces repetitive loading/error state boilerplate across every async operation in an application.
      <pre class="code-block">function useAsync(asyncFn, immediate = false) {
  const [state, setState] = React.useState({
    status: 'idle', data: null, error: null,
  });

  const execute = React.useCallback(async (...args) =&gt; {
    setState({ status: 'loading', data: null, error: null });
    try {
      const data = await asyncFn(...args);
      setState({ status: 'success', data, error: null });
      return data;
    } catch (error) {
      setState({ status: 'idle', data: null, error });
      throw error;
    }
  }, [asyncFn]);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (immediate) execute();
  }, [execute, immediate]);

  return { ...state, execute, isLoading: state.status === 'loading' };
}

// Usage
function UserProfile({ userId }) {
  const { data: user, isLoading, error, execute } = useAsync(
    () =&gt; fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
    true // execute immediately on mount
  );
  if (isLoading) return &lt;Spinner /&gt;;
  if (error) return &lt;Error /&gt;;
  return &lt;Profile user={user} onRefresh={execute} /&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q42">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useInfiniteScroll custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q42" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Infinite scroll loads more content when the user approaches the bottom of the list. The pattern combines <code>IntersectionObserver</code> (to detect when a sentinel element becomes visible) with a data-fetching callback. The hook returns a ref to attach to the sentinel element placed at the bottom of the list. When the sentinel enters the viewport, the observer fires, the hook sets an <code>isFetching</code> guard to prevent concurrent fetches, calls <code>fetchMore</code>, and resets the guard when the fetch completes. Cleanup disconnects the observer to prevent memory leaks and stale callbacks on unmount or URL change.
      <pre class="code-block">function useInfiniteScroll(fetchMore, { threshold = 0.1, rootMargin = '200px' } = {}) {
  const sentinelRef = React.useRef(null);
  const [isFetching, setIsFetching] = React.useState(false);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const sentinel = sentinelRef.current;
    if (!sentinel) return;

    const observer = new IntersectionObserver(
      async ([entry]) =&gt; {
        if (entry.isIntersecting &amp;&amp; !isFetching) {
          setIsFetching(true);
          try { await fetchMore(); }
          finally { setIsFetching(false); }
        }
      },
      { threshold, rootMargin }
    );
    observer.observe(sentinel);
    return () =&gt; observer.disconnect();
  }, [fetchMore, isFetching, threshold, rootMargin]);

  return { sentinelRef, isFetching };
}

// Usage
function PostList() {
  const [posts, setPosts] = React.useState([]);
  const [page, setPage] = React.useState(1);
  const fetchMore = React.useCallback(async () =&gt; {
    const newPosts = await fetchPosts(page + 1);
    setPosts(prev =&gt; [...prev, ...newPosts]);
    setPage(p =&gt; p + 1);
  }, [page]);
  const { sentinelRef, isFetching } = useInfiniteScroll(fetchMore);
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {posts.map(p =&gt; &lt;Post key={p.id} post={p} /&gt;)}
      &lt;div ref={sentinelRef}&gt;{isFetching &amp;&amp; &lt;Spinner /&gt;}&lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q43">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;Explain the difference between controlled and uncontrolled components and when to use refs.
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q43" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Controlled components have their value driven by React state — every change goes through <code>setState</code>, and the input always reflects the state value. Uncontrolled components manage their own value in the DOM and you read the value imperatively using a ref only when needed (e.g., on form submit). Controlled components are the React standard because they enable validation on every keystroke, allow deriving state from input, synchronize multiple related inputs, and give React full control over form data. Uncontrolled components with refs are appropriate when integrating with non-React code, when performance is critical (no re-render per keystroke), or for file inputs which can only be uncontrolled because the browser prevents JavaScript from setting the file value. React Hook Form uses uncontrolled inputs internally for performance, adding a thin React layer for validation — this is why it significantly outperforms fully-controlled form libraries.
      <pre class="code-block">// Controlled: value driven by state
function ControlledInput() {
  const [value, setValue] = React.useState('');
  return (
    &lt;input
      value={value} // React controls the DOM value
      onChange={e =&gt; setValue(e.target.value)}
    /&gt;
  );
}

// Uncontrolled: DOM manages its own value
function UncontrolledInput() {
  const inputRef = React.useRef(null);
  const handleSubmit = () =&gt; {
    console.log(inputRef.current.value); // read imperatively
  };
  return &lt;input ref={inputRef} defaultValue="initial" /&gt;;
}

// File input: always uncontrolled (React can't set the file value)
function FileInput() {
  const fileRef = React.useRef(null);
  const handleUpload = () =&gt; {
    const file = fileRef.current.files[0];
    uploadFile(file);
  };
  return &lt;input type="file" ref={fileRef} /&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q44">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement optimistic updates with hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q44" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Optimistic updates immediately update the UI with the expected result before the server confirms, rolling back if the request fails. This creates a highly responsive feel — the UI responds instantly to user actions without waiting for network round-trips. The pattern has three steps: update local state immediately with the expected result, fire the async request in the background, and revert to the original state if the request fails. A <code>rollback</code> callback captures the previous state via closure at the time of the interaction. Libraries like React Query and SWR provide first-class optimistic update support via their <code>mutate</code> APIs, but the custom hook pattern shown here captures the essential mechanics.
      <pre class="code-block">function useOptimisticUpdate(mutationFn) {
  const [error, setError] = React.useState(null);

  const mutate = React.useCallback(async ({
    optimisticUpdate,
    rollback,
    variables,
  }) =&gt; {
    optimisticUpdate(); // immediately update UI
    setError(null);
    try {
      await mutationFn(variables);
    } catch (err) {
      rollback(); // revert on error
      setError(err);
    }
  }, [mutationFn]);

  return { mutate, error };
}

// Usage: like button
function LikeButton({ post }) {
  const [liked, setLiked] = React.useState(post.liked);
  const [likeCount, setLikeCount] = React.useState(post.likeCount);
  const { mutate } = useOptimisticUpdate(toggleLike);

  const handleLike = () =&gt; {
    mutate({
      optimisticUpdate: () =&gt; { setLiked(l =&gt; !l); setLikeCount(c =&gt; liked ? c - 1 : c + 1); },
      rollback: () =&gt; { setLiked(post.liked); setLikeCount(post.likeCount); },
      variables: { postId: post.id },
    });
  };
  return &lt;button onClick={handleLike}&gt;{liked ? '&#10084;&#65039;' : '&#129293;'} {likeCount}&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q45">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is useContext's performance impact and how do you mitigate it?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q45" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Every component that calls <code>useContext(MyContext)</code> re-renders whenever the Context's <code>value</code> reference changes — even if the specific slice of data that component cares about hasn't changed. This is a significant performance issue when a Context holds a large object and a deeply nested component only uses a small part of it. The first mitigation is to split the context into multiple smaller, focused contexts (user context, theme context, notifications context) so components only subscribe to what they need. The second is to memoize the context value with <code>useMemo</code> to prevent new object references on every parent render. The third is to separate the rarely-changing dispatch from the frequently-changing state into two distinct contexts. For complex cases, Zustand or Redux are preferable because they have built-in selector-based subscriptions that only trigger re-renders when the selected slice changes.
      <pre class="code-block">// Problematic: entire context re-renders all consumers on any change
const AppContext = React.createContext(null);
// Any update to user, theme, or notifications re-renders ALL consumers

// Better: split into focused contexts
const UserContext = React.createContext(null);
const ThemeContext = React.createContext(null);
const NotificationsContext = React.createContext(null);

// Components only subscribe to what they need
function UserAvatar() {
  const user = React.useContext(UserContext); // only re-renders on user changes
  return &lt;img src={user.avatar} /&gt;;
}

// Separate stable dispatch from changing state
function AppProvider({ children }) {
  const [state, dispatch] = React.useReducer(reducer, initialState);
  const stableMemoState = React.useMemo(() =&gt; state, [state]);
  // dispatch is already stable (never changes between renders)
  return (
    &lt;DispatchContext.Provider value={dispatch}&gt;
      &lt;StateContext.Provider value={stableMemoState}&gt;
        {children}
      &lt;/StateContext.Provider&gt;
    &lt;/DispatchContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q46">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a usePrevious custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q46" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>usePrevious</code> stores the previous value of a prop or state between renders, which is useful for detecting the direction of changes, comparing before/after values in animations, or triggering logic only when a value transitions from one specific value to another. The pattern uses a ref (since refs persist between renders without triggering re-renders) and an effect that runs after every render to update the ref to the current value. The ref always holds the value from the previous render during the current render because the effect runs after rendering is committed to the DOM. An alternative implementation updates the ref synchronously during render rather than in an effect, which means it reflects the value from the previous render immediately without needing an effect cycle.
      <pre class="code-block">function usePrevious(value) {
  const ref = React.useRef(undefined);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    ref.current = value; // updates AFTER render, so ref.current is previous value during render
  });

  return ref.current; // returns value from previous render
}

// Usage: detect direction of changes
function PriceDisplay({ price }) {
  const prevPrice = usePrevious(price);
  const direction = prevPrice !== undefined
    ? price &gt; prevPrice ? 'up' : price &lt; prevPrice ? 'down' : 'same'
    : 'same';

  return (
    &lt;span style={{ color: direction === 'up' ? 'green' : direction === 'down' ? 'red' : 'black' }}&gt;
      ${price}
      {direction === 'up' ? ' &#9650;' : direction === 'down' ? ' &#9660;' : ''}
    &lt;/span&gt;
  );
}

// Enhanced version with immediate update on first render
function usePreviousEnhanced(value, initialValue) {
  const ref = React.useRef(initialValue);
  const prev = ref.current;
  ref.current = value;
  return prev; // immediately returns the previous value (no effect needed)
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q47">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the useInsertionEffect hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q47" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useInsertionEffect</code> (React 18+) is a hook designed for CSS-in-JS libraries that need to inject styles into the DOM before any layout effects or browser paint occur. It fires synchronously before DOM mutations — even earlier than <code>useLayoutEffect</code> — giving CSS-in-JS libraries like styled-components and Emotion the opportunity to inject <code>&lt;style&gt;</code> tags before the browser measures layout, avoiding the costly extra layout recalculations that occurred when styles were injected afterward. Application code should never use this hook directly; it is intended solely for CSS-in-JS library authors to solve their specific injection-timing problem. Unlike <code>useLayoutEffect</code>, <code>useInsertionEffect</code> cannot read layout because it runs before layout is even calculated, and it also cannot use refs for the same reason. If you find yourself reaching for this hook in application code, you are likely solving a problem better addressed by <code>useLayoutEffect</code> or <code>useEffect</code>.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q48">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you test custom hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q48" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Custom hooks can be tested using <code>renderHook</code> from <code>@testing-library/react</code>, which renders a minimal host component that calls the hook and exposes its return value via <code>result.current</code>. The <code>act</code> wrapper ensures all state updates and effects are flushed before assertions run, mirroring how React batches updates in a real browser environment. For hooks with async behavior (data fetching, timers), <code>waitFor</code> polls until the assertion passes or times out. MSW (Mock Service Worker) is the recommended tool for mocking API calls within hook tests because it intercepts at the network level, matching real usage more closely than mocking <code>fetch</code> directly. Testing hooks in isolation rather than only through components makes them easier to debug because failures point directly at the hook logic rather than a component's rendering layer.
      <pre class="code-block">import { renderHook, act, waitFor } from '@testing-library/react';
import { useCounter } from './useCounter';
import { useFetch } from './useFetch';

// Test a simple stateful hook
test('useCounter increments correctly', () =&gt; {
  const { result } = renderHook(() =&gt; useCounter(0));

  expect(result.current.count).toBe(0);

  act(() =&gt; { result.current.increment(); });

  expect(result.current.count).toBe(1);
});

// Test a hook with async behavior
test('useFetch returns data', async () =&gt; {
  server.use(rest.get('/api/user', (req, res, ctx) =&gt; res(ctx.json({ name: 'Alice' }))));

  const { result } = renderHook(() =&gt; useFetch('/api/user'));

  expect(result.current.loading).toBe(true);

  await waitFor(() =&gt; expect(result.current.loading).toBe(false));

  expect(result.current.data).toEqual({ name: 'Alice' });
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q49">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useMutation hook pattern?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q49" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A <code>useMutation</code> hook models write operations — unlike queries which fetch data, mutations change data on the server and often need to update local state, invalidate caches, or run side effects on success or error. React Query's <code>useMutation</code> is the canonical production-ready implementation, but understanding the underlying pattern is important. A custom version manages loading, error, and data state and provides an async <code>mutate</code> function that the caller invokes with variables. The hook accepts lifecycle callbacks (<code>onSuccess</code>, <code>onError</code>, <code>onSettled</code>) using optional chaining so they are only called when provided. This mirrors the callback pattern used across most data-fetching libraries.
      <pre class="code-block">function useMutation(mutationFn, options = {}) {
  const { onSuccess, onError, onSettled } = options;
  const [state, setState] = React.useState({
    data: null, error: null, status: 'idle',
  });

  const mutate = React.useCallback(async (variables) =&gt; {
    setState({ data: null, error: null, status: 'loading' });
    try {
      const data = await mutationFn(variables);
      setState({ data, error: null, status: 'success' });
      onSuccess?.(data, variables);
      onSettled?.(data, null, variables);
      return data;
    } catch (error) {
      setState({ data: null, error, status: 'error' });
      onError?.(error, variables);
      onSettled?.(null, error, variables);
      throw error;
    }
  }, [mutationFn, onSuccess, onError, onSettled]);

  return { ...state, mutate, isLoading: state.status === 'loading' };
}

// Usage
function CreatePost() {
  const { mutate, isLoading } = useMutation(createPost, {
    onSuccess: (post) =&gt; navigate(`/posts/${post.id}`),
    onError: (err) =&gt; showToast(`Error: ${err.message}`),
  });
  return &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; mutate({ title: 'New Post' })} disabled={isLoading}&gt;Create&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q50">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the useReducer + Context pattern for global state?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q50" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The <code>useReducer</code> + <code>Context</code> pattern is a lightweight Redux alternative for medium-complexity global state that benefits from structured, predictable state transitions. A single reducer manages all state transitions via typed action objects, making the data flow explicit and debuggable. State and dispatch are exposed through separate contexts — dispatch never changes between renders so components that only need to dispatch don't re-render when state changes. Custom hooks wrap the <code>useContext</code> calls to provide a clean, ergonomic API and to throw helpful errors when used outside the provider. This pattern excels for shopping carts, wizard forms, theme management, and application settings — anywhere you want reducer-style state management without adding Redux to the dependency tree.
      <pre class="code-block">// store.js
const StateContext = React.createContext(null);
const DispatchContext = React.createContext(null);

const initialState = { cart: [], user: null, theme: 'light' };

function appReducer(state, action) {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'ADD_TO_CART': return { ...state, cart: [...state.cart, action.item] };
    case 'SET_USER': return { ...state, user: action.user };
    case 'TOGGLE_THEME': return { ...state, theme: state.theme === 'light' ? 'dark' : 'light' };
    default: return state;
  }
}

export function AppProvider({ children }) {
  const [state, dispatch] = React.useReducer(appReducer, initialState);
  return (
    &lt;DispatchContext.Provider value={dispatch}&gt;
      &lt;StateContext.Provider value={state}&gt;
        {children}
      &lt;/StateContext.Provider&gt;
    &lt;/DispatchContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

// Custom hooks for clean API
export const useAppState = () =&gt; React.useContext(StateContext);
export const useAppDispatch = () =&gt; React.useContext(DispatchContext);

// Usage
function CartButton() {
  const { cart } = useAppState();
  const dispatch = useAppDispatch();
  return &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'ADD_TO_CART', item: { id: 1 } })}&gt;
    Cart ({cart.length})
  &lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q51">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle side effects that should only run once?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q51" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Use <code>useEffect</code> with an empty dependency array <code>[]</code> to run an effect only once after the initial mount, which is React's functional equivalent of <code>componentDidMount</code>. The effect's cleanup function runs when the component unmounts, making it the right place to set up and tear down long-lived resources like event listeners or WebSocket connections. However, React 18's StrictMode intentionally mounts, unmounts, and remounts components in development to surface side effects that aren't properly cleaned up — so even a <code>[]</code> effect runs twice in development, which is by design. This double-mount behavior does not occur in production builds. To ensure correctness, always write cleanup functions and make effects idempotent. If you truly need setup that runs exactly once per application lifecycle rather than per component mount, place that code outside any component at module level.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q52">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between useState and useRef for tracking values?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q52" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Both <code>useState</code> and <code>useRef</code> can persist values across renders, but they differ in one critical way: <code>useState</code> triggers a re-render when its value changes, while <code>useRef</code> does not trigger any re-render when <code>ref.current</code> is mutated. Use <code>useState</code> when a value change needs to update the rendered output — for anything the user sees. Use <code>useRef</code> when you need to track a value without causing re-renders, such as whether a component is currently mounted, an interval or timeout ID, the latest version of a callback for use inside an effect, or a DOM element reference. A common mistake is using <code>useRef</code> for values that should drive the UI — the component will never re-render when the ref changes, so the display will be stale. The opposite mistake is using <code>useState</code> for interval IDs or other bookkeeping values — this causes unnecessary re-renders and can trigger infinite loops when the state update is inside an effect.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q53">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useToggle custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q53" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useToggle</code> is a simple but frequently-used custom hook that manages a boolean state and provides stable <code>toggle</code>, <code>setTrue</code>, and <code>setFalse</code> callbacks. It is one of the most commonly written custom hooks in React codebases because boolean state (open/closed, visible/hidden, enabled/disabled) appears in nearly every feature. Memoizing the callbacks with <code>useCallback</code> and empty dependency arrays ensures the function references are stable across renders, preventing child components wrapped in <code>React.memo</code> from re-rendering unnecessarily when the parent re-renders for unrelated reasons. The hook returns both the value and the controls as a tuple, following the same convention as <code>useState</code>.
      <pre class="code-block">function useToggle(initialValue = false) {
  const [value, setValue] = React.useState(initialValue);

  const toggle = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setValue(v =&gt; !v), []);
  const setTrue = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setValue(true), []);
  const setFalse = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setValue(false), []);

  return [value, { toggle, setTrue, setFalse }];
}

// Usage
function Modal() {
  const [isOpen, { toggle, setFalse }] = useToggle(false);
  return (
    &lt;&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={toggle}&gt;Open Modal&lt;/button&gt;
      {isOpen &amp;&amp; (
        &lt;dialog open&gt;
          &lt;p&gt;Modal content&lt;/p&gt;
          &lt;button onClick={setFalse}&gt;Close&lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/dialog&gt;
      )}
    &lt;/&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q54">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the pattern for subscribing to browser APIs with hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q54" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Many browser APIs expose subscription patterns — online/offline status, geolocation, media queries, clipboard, battery, and resize events. The custom hook pattern for all of them is structurally identical: subscribe in <code>useEffect</code>, update state in the subscription callback, and clean up the subscription in the effect's return function. Always initialize state with the current value synchronously to avoid a flash of incorrect state on mount. React 18's <code>useSyncExternalStore</code> is the officially recommended hook for external store subscriptions because it provides concurrent-mode-safe reads and handles server-side rendering snapshots via a third argument.
      <pre class="code-block">// useMediaQuery: subscribe to a CSS media query
function useMediaQuery(query) {
  const [matches, setMatches] = React.useState(
    () =&gt; window.matchMedia(query).matches
  );

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const mediaQuery = window.matchMedia(query);
    const handler = (e) =&gt; setMatches(e.matches);
    mediaQuery.addEventListener('change', handler);
    return () =&gt; mediaQuery.removeEventListener('change', handler);
  }, [query]);

  return matches;
}

// useOnlineStatus: subscribe to navigator.onLine
function useOnlineStatus() {
  return React.useSyncExternalStore(
    (callback) =&gt; {
      window.addEventListener('online', callback);
      window.addEventListener('offline', callback);
      return () =&gt; {
        window.removeEventListener('online', callback);
        window.removeEventListener('offline', callback);
      };
    },
    () =&gt; navigator.onLine,
    () =&gt; true
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q55">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you share logic between hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q55" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Hooks compose naturally — custom hooks can call other custom hooks, and this composition is the primary mechanism for sharing and reusing logic across components. A <code>useUserProfile</code> hook might internally call <code>useFetch</code>, <code>useLocalStorage</code>, and <code>useErrorHandler</code>, combining their outputs into a single coherent interface. Each individual hook remains independently testable, reusable in different contexts, and focused on a single concern. The only constraint is that custom hooks must call other hooks unconditionally (following the Rules of Hooks), so conditional composition must be achieved by passing parameters to a hook rather than conditionally calling the hook. Building up a vocabulary of domain-specific hooks — <code>useCurrentUser</code>, <code>useFeatureFlag</code>, <code>usePermission</code>, <code>useOptimisticList</code> — makes business logic portable and the components that use them declarative.
      <pre class="code-block">// Composing custom hooks
function useUserProfile(userId) {
  const { data, loading, error } = useFetch(`/api/users/${userId}`);
  const [savedProfile, setSavedProfile] = useLocalStorage(`profile-${userId}`, null);
  const isOnline = useOnlineStatus();

  // Merge fetched data with cached data
  const profile = React.useMemo(() =&gt;
    data ?? savedProfile,
    [data, savedProfile]
  );

  // Cache the fetched data
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (data) setSavedProfile(data);
  }, [data, setSavedProfile]);

  return { profile, loading, error, isOnline };
}

// usePermission composes useCurrentUser + feature flags
function usePermission(permission) {
  const user = useCurrentUser();
  const flags = useFeatureFlags();
  return React.useMemo(() =&gt;
    checkPermission(user, permission, flags),
    [user, permission, flags]
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q56">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the pattern for managing subscriptions with useEffect?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q56" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Subscriptions such as WebSockets, EventEmitter, RxJS observables, and Server-Sent Event streams all share the same lifecycle pattern: subscribe when the component mounts or when a key dependency changes, update React state when data arrives, and clean up when the component unmounts or dependencies change. The cleanup function returned from <code>useEffect</code> is critical — failing to unsubscribe causes memory leaks where the subscription continues to run and attempts to call <code>setState</code> on an unmounted component. The dependency array should contain the parameters that identify the subscription (such as a room ID or channel name) so the effect automatically reconnects when they change. This pattern is consistent regardless of the subscription mechanism — only the specific API calls differ.
      <pre class="code-block">// WebSocket subscription hook
function useWebSocket(url) {
  const [messages, setMessages] = React.useState([]);
  const [status, setStatus] = React.useState('connecting');
  const wsRef = React.useRef(null);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const ws = new WebSocket(url);
    wsRef.current = ws;

    ws.onopen = () =&gt; setStatus('connected');
    ws.onclose = () =&gt; setStatus('disconnected');
    ws.onerror = () =&gt; setStatus('error');
    ws.onmessage = (e) =&gt; {
      const msg = JSON.parse(e.data);
      setMessages(prev =&gt; [...prev, msg]);
    };

    return () =&gt; {
      ws.close(); // clean up -- unsubscribe
      wsRef.current = null;
    };
  }, [url]); // reconnect if URL changes

  const send = React.useCallback((msg) =&gt; {
    wsRef.current?.send(JSON.stringify(msg));
  }, []);

  return { messages, status, send };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q57">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useWhyDidYouUpdate debugging hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q57" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useWhyDidYouUpdate</code> logs which props or values changed between renders — it is a development-only debugging hook useful for diagnosing unexpected re-renders in memoized or pure components. It stores the previous values in a ref (because refs persist without triggering re-renders) and compares them to the current values after each render. Any keys whose values differ between the previous and current render are collected and logged to the console with their before/after values. This hook is particularly valuable when you've wrapped a component with <code>React.memo</code> and it's still re-rendering — <code>useWhyDidYouUpdate</code> will tell you exactly which prop is failing the shallow equality check. The hook should be removed or guarded behind a development flag before deploying to production.
      <pre class="code-block">function useWhyDidYouUpdate(name, props) {
  const previousProps = React.useRef({});

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (previousProps.current) {
      const allKeys = Object.keys({ ...previousProps.current, ...props });
      const changedProps = {};

      allKeys.forEach(key =&gt; {
        if (previousProps.current[key] !== props[key]) {
          changedProps[key] = {
            from: previousProps.current[key],
            to: props[key],
          };
        }
      });

      if (Object.keys(changedProps).length) {
        console.log('[WHY DID YOU UPDATE]', name, changedProps);
      }
    }
    previousProps.current = props;
  });
}

// Usage -- add to any component to debug re-renders
function ExpensiveComponent(props) {
  useWhyDidYouUpdate('ExpensiveComponent', props);
  // ... rest of component
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q58">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the pattern for using hooks with TypeScript generics?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q58" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      TypeScript generics in custom hooks allow type-safe reusable hooks where the generic type parameter flows through the hook's internal state and return types, ensuring type safety without sacrificing reusability. Without generics, a fetch hook would be forced to return <code>unknown</code> or <code>any</code>, losing all type information about the data shape. With generics, callers provide the expected response type and TypeScript infers it throughout — the IDE provides autocomplete on the returned data, and TypeScript catches attempts to access non-existent properties at compile time. Properly typed hooks are one of the strongest arguments for using TypeScript in a React codebase because they eliminate entire categories of runtime errors at the hook boundary.
      <pre class="code-block">// Generic useFetch hook
interface FetchState&lt;T&gt; {
  data: T | null;
  loading: boolean;
  error: string | null;
}

function useFetch&lt;T&gt;(url: string): FetchState&lt;T&gt; {
  const [state, setState] = React.useState&lt;FetchState&lt;T&gt;&gt;({
    data: null, loading: true, error: null,
  });

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    let cancelled = false;
    fetch(url)
      .then(r =&gt; r.json() as Promise&lt;T&gt;)
      .then(data =&gt; { if (!cancelled) setState({ data, loading: false, error: null }); })
      .catch(err =&gt; { if (!cancelled) setState({ data: null, loading: false, error: err.message }); });
    return () =&gt; { cancelled = true; };
  }, [url]);

  return state;
}

// Usage: TypeScript infers the type
interface User { id: number; name: string; }
function Profile({ id }: { id: number }) {
  const { data, loading } = useFetch&lt;User&gt;(`/api/users/${id}`);
  // data is typed as User | null -- TypeScript knows its shape
  if (loading) return &lt;Spinner /&gt;;
  return &lt;p&gt;{data?.name}&lt;/p&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q59">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle derived state correctly with hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q59" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Derived state is data that can be computed from other existing state or props, and it should almost never be stored in its own <code>useState</code> variable because doing so creates a synchronization problem — the derived state can fall out of sync with the source data whenever the source changes. Instead, compute cheap derivations directly during render (they recalculate on every render automatically), and wrap expensive derivations in <code>useMemo</code> to cache the result until the source data changes. The classic anti-pattern is storing <code>fullName = firstName + ' ' + lastName</code> in state and then writing an effect to keep it updated — this is fragile, adds unnecessary complexity, and adds extra render cycles. The principle is: one source of truth per piece of data, and everything else is computed.
      <pre class="code-block">// Anti-pattern: derived state stored in useState
function UserDisplay({ user }) {
  const [fullName, setFullName] = React.useState(user.firstName + ' ' + user.lastName);
  // fullName gets out of sync if user prop changes!

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    setFullName(user.firstName + ' ' + user.lastName); // must manually sync
  }, [user.firstName, user.lastName]);
}

// Correct: compute during render
function UserDisplayFixed({ user }) {
  const fullName = user.firstName + ' ' + user.lastName; // always in sync

  // For expensive derivations, use useMemo
  const sortedOrders = React.useMemo(() =&gt;
    [...user.orders].sort((a, b) =&gt; b.date - a.date),
    [user.orders]
  );
  return &lt;div&gt;&lt;h1&gt;{fullName}&lt;/h1&gt;&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q60">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;You need to synchronize with an external system (e.g., analytics, third-party SDK). How would you structure this with hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q60" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      External system synchronization — analytics events, third-party SDKs, charting libraries, mapping APIs — should be encapsulated in a custom hook that initializes the external system, keeps it synchronized with React state and prop changes, and cleans up properly on unmount. The key principle is keeping the external system's imperative API entirely behind a clean hook interface so the consuming component remains declarative. Use <code>useRef</code> to hold the external system instance because it is mutable bookkeeping, not state that drives rendering. Use <code>useLayoutEffect</code> for initialization when the external system needs to measure DOM dimensions on setup, and <code>useEffect</code> for data synchronization which can run after paint. Separate the initialization effect from the data-synchronization effect so data updates don't re-create the external instance unnecessarily.
      <pre class="code-block">// Encapsulating a third-party chart library
function useChart(containerRef, data, options) {
  const chartRef = React.useRef(null);

  // Initialize chart on mount
  React.useLayoutEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (!containerRef.current) return;
    chartRef.current = new ThirdPartyChart(containerRef.current, options);
    return () =&gt; {
      chartRef.current?.destroy(); // clean up
      chartRef.current = null;
    };
  }, []); // only on mount

  // Synchronize data changes
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    chartRef.current?.updateData(data);
  }, [data]);

  // Synchronize option changes
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    chartRef.current?.setOptions(options);
  }, [options]);
}

// Usage: component stays declarative
function SalesChart({ salesData, chartOptions }) {
  const containerRef = React.useRef(null);
  useChart(containerRef, salesData, chartOptions);
  return &lt;div ref={containerRef} style={{ height: 400 }} /&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q61">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;A hook you're using seems to cause an infinite re-render loop. How do you debug and fix it?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q61" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Systematic debugging approach for infinite re-render loops: (1) Open React DevTools, navigate to the Components tab, and look for a component showing a rapidly increasing render count; (2) Add <code>console.log('render', deps)</code> just before the suspect <code>useEffect</code> to log each time it fires and which dependency values triggered it; (3) Log each dependency value individually to find which one is a new reference on every render; (4) The most common culprit is an object, array, or function created inline in the component body used as a dependency — it gets a new reference identity on every render even if its contents are the same; (5) Check if the effect updates state that appears in its own dependency array, creating a cycle where the effect triggers a re-render which triggers the effect again; (6) Fix by moving the value to module-level constant if it never changes, wrapping it with <code>useMemo</code> or <code>useCallback</code> for stable identity, or using the functional updater form of <code>setState</code> to remove the state variable from the dependency array. The <code>eslint-plugin-react-hooks</code> <code>exhaustive-deps</code> rule prevents most of these issues at development time by flagging missing or incorrectly specified dependencies.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q62">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How would you implement a custom hook for real-time collaborative features?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q62" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Real-time collaboration requires connecting to a persistent channel (WebSocket or SSE), receiving document updates from other users, sending local edits to the server, and maintaining presence state showing who else is currently viewing the document. The hook encapsulates the entire connection lifecycle — establishing the connection, parsing message types, dispatching state updates, and sending outgoing messages — so the component only needs to render the document state and call <code>sendEdit</code> when the user makes changes. Reconnection logic, heartbeat pings, and exponential backoff for connection failures are production concerns that would be added to the hook without changing the component API. The <code>userId</code> dependency in the effect ensures a fresh connection is established if the current user changes.
      <pre class="code-block">function useCollaboration(documentId, userId) {
  const [document, setDocument] = React.useState(null);
  const [collaborators, setCollaborators] = React.useState([]);
  const wsRef = React.useRef(null);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const ws = new WebSocket(`wss://api.example.com/docs/${documentId}`);

    ws.onopen = () =&gt; ws.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'JOIN', userId }));

    ws.onmessage = (e) =&gt; {
      const msg = JSON.parse(e.data);
      switch (msg.type) {
        case 'DOCUMENT_UPDATE':
          setDocument(msg.document);
          break;
        case 'COLLABORATOR_JOINED':
        case 'COLLABORATOR_LEFT':
          setCollaborators(msg.collaborators);
          break;
      }
    };

    wsRef.current = ws;
    return () =&gt; { ws.close(); wsRef.current = null; };
  }, [documentId, userId]);

  const sendEdit = React.useCallback((edit) =&gt; {
    wsRef.current?.send(JSON.stringify({ type: 'EDIT', edit, userId }));
  }, [userId]);

  return { document, collaborators, sendEdit };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q63">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between useEffect and event handlers for responding to user interactions?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q63" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The rule of thumb from the React team is: event handlers are for responding to user interactions; <code>useEffect</code> is for synchronizing with external systems. Do not use <code>useEffect</code> to handle the downstream consequences of a user interaction — handle them directly in the event handler itself. Putting interaction logic in an effect that watches a state flag creates indirect, hard-to-trace data flows that require reading two separate locations (the event handler setting the flag, and the effect reacting to it) to understand what happens when a button is clicked. The React documentation now explicitly states: "If you're not synchronizing with an external system, you probably don't need an Effect." Effects are the right tool for: data fetching on mount, subscriptions, DOM manipulation, and third-party integrations. They are not the right tool for: computing derived state from other state, handling user events, or chaining state updates that could be computed in a single event handler.
      <pre class="code-block">// Anti-pattern: using useEffect to respond to user interaction
function Form() {
  const [submitted, setSubmitted] = React.useState(false);
  const [result, setResult] = React.useState(null);

  // Effect watches submitted state to trigger submission -- indirect and confusing
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (submitted) {
      submitForm(data).then(setResult);
    }
  }, [submitted]);
}

// Correct: handle interaction directly in event handler
function FormFixed() {
  const [result, setResult] = React.useState(null);

  const handleSubmit = async (e) =&gt; {
    e.preventDefault();
    const result = await submitForm(data); // direct, traceable, no indirection
    setResult(result);
  };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q64">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useMap custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q64" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useMap</code> provides a React-aware wrapper around JavaScript's built-in <code>Map</code>, giving you <code>set</code>, <code>delete</code>, and <code>clear</code> operations that trigger re-renders when called. The challenge is that mutating a <code>Map</code> in place doesn't change its reference, so React's shallow equality check in state wouldn't detect the change — you must create a new <code>Map</code> on every operation. All three mutation callbacks are memoized with <code>useCallback</code> and empty dependency arrays so their references are stable, making them safe to pass as props to memoized child components. The hook also exposes pass-through read methods like <code>has</code> and <code>get</code> that delegate directly to the underlying Map without going through state.
      <pre class="code-block">function useMap(initialEntries = []) {
  const [map, setMap] = React.useState(() =&gt; new Map(initialEntries));

  const set = React.useCallback((key, value) =&gt; {
    setMap(prev =&gt; new Map([...prev, [key, value]]));
  }, []);

  const remove = React.useCallback((key) =&gt; {
    setMap(prev =&gt; { const next = new Map(prev); next.delete(key); return next; });
  }, []);

  const clear = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setMap(new Map()), []);

  return { map, set, delete: remove, clear, has: (key) =&gt; map.has(key), get: (key) =&gt; map.get(key) };
}

// Usage: track selected items
function ItemSelector({ items }) {
  const { map: selected, set, delete: deselect } = useMap();

  const toggle = (item) =&gt;
    selected.has(item.id) ? deselect(item.id) : set(item.id, item);

  return (
    &lt;ul&gt;
      {items.map(item =&gt; (
        &lt;li key={item.id} onClick={() =&gt; toggle(item)} style={{ fontWeight: selected.has(item.id) ? 'bold' : 'normal' }}&gt;
          {item.name}
        &lt;/li&gt;
      ))}
    &lt;/ul&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q65">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useSet custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q65" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useSet</code> wraps JavaScript's <code>Set</code> with React state management, providing stable <code>add</code>, <code>remove</code>, <code>toggle</code>, <code>clear</code>, and <code>reset</code> operations that each trigger re-renders by replacing the Set with a new instance. Like <code>useMap</code>, the underlying principle is immutability — a mutated Set has the same reference as before, so React would not detect the change, meaning every operation must return a new Set. The <code>toggle</code> function is a particularly useful convenience that adds the item if it's absent or removes it if it's present, covering the common multi-select and tag-selection use case in a single operation. The <code>reset</code> function restores the Set to its initial values, which is useful in forms that can be cleared back to defaults.
      <pre class="code-block">function useSet(initialValues = []) {
  const [set, setSet] = React.useState(() =&gt; new Set(initialValues));

  const add = React.useCallback((item) =&gt; {
    setSet(prev =&gt; new Set([...prev, item]));
  }, []);

  const remove = React.useCallback((item) =&gt; {
    setSet(prev =&gt; { const next = new Set(prev); next.delete(item); return next; });
  }, []);

  const toggle = React.useCallback((item) =&gt; {
    setSet(prev =&gt; {
      const next = new Set(prev);
      next.has(item) ? next.delete(item) : next.add(item);
      return next;
    });
  }, []);

  const clear = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setSet(new Set()), []);
  const reset = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setSet(new Set(initialValues)), [initialValues]);

  return { set, add, remove, toggle, clear, reset, has: (item) =&gt; set.has(item), size: set.size };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q66">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What does React.StrictMode do to hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q66" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React <code>StrictMode</code> double-invokes certain functions in development — component render bodies, state initializer functions, reducer functions, and the callbacks passed to <code>useState</code> — and additionally mounts, unmounts, and remounts each component so that <code>useEffect</code> runs twice (mount, cleanup, mount again). This intentional double-invocation is designed to surface bugs that would otherwise only appear under specific conditions: non-idempotent renders with side effects in the render body, missing effect cleanup functions that cause resource leaks, impure reducers that mutate state directly rather than returning new state, and stale closure issues where an effect captures an outdated reference. None of these behaviors occur in production — <code>StrictMode</code> is purely a development tool. If your application breaks under <code>StrictMode</code>, it means you have real bugs that <code>StrictMode</code> is surfacing early, and those bugs must be fixed rather than worked around.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q67">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the relationship between hooks and the React Fiber architecture?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q67" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Fiber is React's internal reconciliation engine, rewritten from scratch in React 16, which replaced the original stack-based reconciler. Hooks are implemented as a singly linked list of hook nodes attached to each Fiber node — the Fiber for a component instance holds a pointer to the first hook, and each hook node has a pointer to the next. This linked-list implementation is why the Rules of Hooks exist: on every render, React iterates the hooks linked list in order, matching the Nth call to <code>useState</code> with the Nth hook node stored on the fiber. If hooks are called conditionally, the number or order of hook calls can change between renders, causing React to incorrectly match a hook call to the wrong node — resulting in state corruption and unpredictable behavior. The Fiber architecture also enables React's concurrent features by making rendering interruptible — React can pause work on a low-priority tree, process a high-priority user interaction, and then resume the paused work, which was impossible with the synchronous call-stack model of the old reconciler.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q68">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useResizeObserver custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q68" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>ResizeObserver</code> efficiently detects when an individual element's size changes, firing a callback with the new dimensions. Unlike window resize event listeners, <code>ResizeObserver</code> works on any element and fires only when that specific element's dimensions change, making it much more performant for responsive component logic. This is particularly valuable for charts, canvases, virtual lists, and any component that needs to render differently based on the exact available space. The hook attaches the observer in a <code>useEffect</code> and disconnects it in the cleanup function to prevent memory leaks. Reading the element's initial size is handled by storing the first observation callback result in state.
      <pre class="code-block">function useResizeObserver(ref) {
  const [size, setSize] = React.useState({ width: 0, height: 0 });

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (!ref.current) return;

    const observer = new ResizeObserver(([entry]) =&gt; {
      const { width, height } = entry.contentRect;
      setSize({ width, height });
    });

    observer.observe(ref.current);
    return () =&gt; observer.disconnect();
  }, [ref]);

  return size;
}

// Usage: chart that fills its container
function ResponsiveChart({ data }) {
  const containerRef = React.useRef(null);
  const { width, height } = useResizeObserver(containerRef);

  return (
    &lt;div ref={containerRef} style={{ width: '100%', height: '100%' }}&gt;
      {width &gt; 0 &amp;&amp; (
        &lt;svg width={width} height={height}&gt;
          {/* render chart using exact container dimensions */}
          &lt;ChartContent data={data} width={width} height={height} /&gt;
        &lt;/svg&gt;
      )}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q69">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the use() hook in React 19?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q69" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>use(resource)</code> is a new React 19 primitive that reads the value of a Promise or Context during rendering. Unlike all other hooks, <code>use</code> can be called inside loops and conditionals because it is not based on call order position in the hooks linked list. For Promises: <code>use(promise)</code> suspends the component while the promise is pending (triggering the nearest Suspense boundary's fallback), throws if it rejects (caught by the nearest Error Boundary), and returns the resolved value synchronously once resolved — enabling async/await-like patterns inside component render functions. For Context: <code>use(Context)</code> works like <code>useContext</code> but can be called inside conditionals, providing more flexibility. Combined with React Server Components and serialized promises passed from server to client, <code>use</code> is the foundation of React 19's streaming data model where the server can send promise placeholders that the client resolves as data arrives.
      <pre class="code-block">// React 19: use() with a Promise
function UserProfile({ userPromise }) {
  const user = React.use(userPromise); // suspends until promise resolves
  // by the time we get here, user is the resolved value
  return &lt;div&gt;{user.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}

// Parent wraps with Suspense + ErrorBoundary
function App({ userId }) {
  const userPromise = fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`).then(r =&gt; r.json());
  return (
    &lt;ErrorBoundary fallback={&lt;ErrorPage /&gt;}&gt;
      &lt;Suspense fallback={&lt;Skeleton /&gt;}&gt;
        &lt;UserProfile userPromise={userPromise} /&gt;
      &lt;/Suspense&gt;
    &lt;/ErrorBoundary&gt;
  );
}

// use() with Context -- can be called conditionally!
function ThemedButton({ showTheme }) {
  if (showTheme) {
    const theme = React.use(ThemeContext); // conditional context read
    return &lt;button style={{ background: theme.primary }}&gt;Themed&lt;/button&gt;;
  }
  return &lt;button&gt;Default&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q70">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How would you architect a custom hook for a complex feature like a drag-and-drop list?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q70" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Drag-and-drop involves tracking the drag source index, the drag-over target index, and computing the new item order when the drag ends. This can be implemented with the HTML5 Drag and Drop API, which is built into browsers and requires no external libraries for basic use cases. A custom hook encapsulates all the event handlers and the reordering logic, returning the current items array and a <code>getItemProps</code> factory function that components spread onto each list item to wire up the drag events. The drag source and target indices are stored in refs rather than state because they don't need to trigger re-renders during the drag — only the final drop triggers a state update with the new order. Preventing the default <code>dragover</code> behavior is required by the browser to allow the <code>drop</code> event to fire.
      <pre class="code-block">function useDragAndDrop(initialItems) {
  const [items, setItems] = React.useState(initialItems);
  const dragItem = React.useRef(null);
  const dragOverItem = React.useRef(null);

  const handleDragStart = React.useCallback((index) =&gt; {
    dragItem.current = index;
  }, []);

  const handleDragEnter = React.useCallback((index) =&gt; {
    dragOverItem.current = index;
  }, []);

  const handleDrop = React.useCallback(() =&gt; {
    if (dragItem.current === null || dragOverItem.current === null) return;
    setItems(prevItems =&gt; {
      const newItems = [...prevItems];
      const [removed] = newItems.splice(dragItem.current, 1);
      newItems.splice(dragOverItem.current, 0, removed);
      return newItems;
    });
    dragItem.current = null;
    dragOverItem.current = null;
  }, []);

  const getItemProps = React.useCallback((index) =&gt; ({
    draggable: true,
    onDragStart: () =&gt; handleDragStart(index),
    onDragEnter: () =&gt; handleDragEnter(index),
    onDragEnd: handleDrop,
    onDragOver: (e) =&gt; e.preventDefault(),
  }), [handleDragStart, handleDragEnter, handleDrop]);

  return { items, getItemProps };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q71">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How would you implement a hook for managing notifications/toasts?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q71" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A toast notification system needs a global queue of messages accessible from anywhere in the component tree, auto-dismiss timers that remove toasts after a configurable duration, and a mechanism for any component to enqueue a new toast without prop drilling. The pattern uses Context plus <code>useReducer</code> for the queue — <code>useReducer</code> is preferred over <code>useState</code> here because the ADD and REMOVE action types make the state transitions explicit and debuggable. The <code>ToastProvider</code> component renders a <code>ToastContainer</code> for display and provides <code>addToast</code> through context. A custom <code>useToast</code> hook wraps the context consumption and throws a helpful error if used outside the provider. Auto-dismiss is handled by scheduling a <code>setTimeout</code> inside <code>addToast</code> rather than in an effect, which is simpler and avoids dependency on the toast's ID in an effect's closure.
      <pre class="code-block">const ToastContext = React.createContext(null);

function toastReducer(state, action) {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'ADD': return [...state, { ...action.toast, id: Date.now() }];
    case 'REMOVE': return state.filter(t =&gt; t.id !== action.id);
    default: return state;
  }
}

export function ToastProvider({ children }) {
  const [toasts, dispatch] = React.useReducer(toastReducer, []);

  const addToast = React.useCallback((message, type = 'info', duration = 3000) =&gt; {
    const id = Date.now();
    dispatch({ type: 'ADD', toast: { message, type, id } });
    setTimeout(() =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'REMOVE', id }), duration);
  }, []);

  return (
    &lt;ToastContext.Provider value={{ toasts, addToast }}&gt;
      {children}
      &lt;ToastContainer toasts={toasts} onDismiss={(id) =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'REMOVE', id })} /&gt;
    &lt;/ToastContext.Provider&gt;
  );
}

export const useToast = () =&gt; {
  const context = React.useContext(ToastContext);
  if (!context) throw new Error('useToast must be used within ToastProvider');
  return context.addToast;
};

// Usage anywhere in the app
function SaveButton() {
  const addToast = useToast();
  const handleSave = async () =&gt; {
    await save();
    addToast('Saved successfully!', 'success');
  };
  return &lt;button onClick={handleSave}&gt;Save&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q72">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useAnimationFrame custom hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q72" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>requestAnimationFrame</code> schedules a callback to run just before the browser's next repaint, making it the correct tool for smooth 60fps animations, canvas rendering, and any value interpolation that needs to be perfectly synchronized with the display refresh rate. A custom hook wrapping it should accept a callback and an <code>isRunning</code flag, use a ref to hold the latest callback to avoid adding it to the effect dependency array (which would restart the loop on every render), and clean up by canceling the pending frame with <code>cancelAnimationFrame</code> on unmount or when <code>isRunning</code> becomes false. Passing <code>deltaTime</code> (milliseconds since the last frame) and the current <code>time</code> to the callback enables frame-rate-independent animations that look consistent on both 60Hz and 120Hz displays.
      <pre class="code-block">function useAnimationFrame(callback, isRunning = true) {
  const callbackRef = React.useRef(callback);
  React.useLayoutEffect(() =&gt; { callbackRef.current = callback; });

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (!isRunning) return;

    let frameId;
    let lastTime = performance.now();

    const loop = (time) =&gt; {
      const deltaTime = time - lastTime;
      lastTime = time;
      callbackRef.current(deltaTime, time);
      frameId = requestAnimationFrame(loop);
    };

    frameId = requestAnimationFrame(loop);
    return () =&gt; cancelAnimationFrame(frameId);
  }, [isRunning]);
}

// Usage: animated counter
function AnimatedValue({ targetValue }) {
  const [displayValue, setDisplayValue] = React.useState(0);
  const startValue = React.useRef(0);
  const startTime = React.useRef(null);
  const duration = 1000; // 1 second

  useAnimationFrame((_, time) =&gt; {
    if (!startTime.current) startTime.current = time;
    const progress = Math.min((time - startTime.current) / duration, 1);
    setDisplayValue(Math.round(startValue.current + (targetValue - startValue.current) * progress));
  }, displayValue !== targetValue);

  return &lt;span&gt;{displayValue}&lt;/span&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q73">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is useActionState in React 19?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q73" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useActionState(action, initialState, permalink?)</code> is a React 19 hook that manages state returned from a form action, returning a <code>[state, formAction, isPending]</code> tuple. The <code>action</code> function receives the previous state and the <code>FormData</code> as arguments and returns the new state — either a success result or an error object. This design is particularly powerful when combined with React Server Components and HTML5 form <code>action</code> attributes, enabling progressive enhancement where the form submits and shows results even without JavaScript. The <code>isPending</code> boolean is true while the action is executing, allowing the submit button to be disabled or show a loading indicator without any manual state management.
