Combined with <code>useFormStatus</code> (used inside the submit button itself to read the form's pending state), React 19 provides a complete, ergonomic form handling solution with no custom state required.
      <pre class="code-block">// React 19: useActionState with server action
import { useActionState } from 'react';

async function submitContactForm(prevState, formData) {
  const email = formData.get('email');
  try {
    await sendEmail(email);
    return { success: true, error: null };
  } catch (err) {
    return { success: false, error: err.message };
  }
}

function ContactForm() {
  const [state, formAction, isPending] = useActionState(submitContactForm, {
    success: false, error: null,
  });

  return (
    &lt;form action={formAction}&gt;
      {state.success &amp;&amp; &lt;p&gt;Message sent!&lt;/p&gt;}
      {state.error &amp;&amp; &lt;p style={{ color: 'red' }}&gt;{state.error}&lt;/p&gt;}
      &lt;input name="email" type="email" required /&gt;
      &lt;button type="submit" disabled={isPending}&gt;
        {isPending ? 'Sending...' : 'Send'}
      &lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q74">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement pagination with hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q74" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Pagination state management involves tracking the current page, deriving total pages from total item count and page size, and providing stable navigation callbacks. A <code>usePagination</code> hook accepts configuration and returns navigation controls along with metadata like <code>startItem</code>, <code>endItem</code>, and <code>canGoNext</code>/<code>canGoPrev</code> flags so the consuming component only needs to render the data. The <code>goToPage</code> function clamps the input to the valid range to prevent invalid page states. A particularly useful derived value is the <code>pageNumbers</code> array — the logic to generate ellipsis-based page number ranges (e.g., 1 ... 4 5 6 ... 20) is complex enough to warrant its own <code>useMemo</code>, and having it inside the hook keeps the component clean.
      <pre class="code-block">function usePagination({ totalItems, initialPage = 1, pageSize = 10 }) {
  const [currentPage, setCurrentPage] = React.useState(initialPage);
  const totalPages = Math.ceil(totalItems / pageSize);

  const goToPage = React.useCallback((page) =&gt; {
    setCurrentPage(Math.max(1, Math.min(page, totalPages)));
  }, [totalPages]);

  const nextPage = React.useCallback(() =&gt; goToPage(currentPage + 1), [currentPage, goToPage]);
  const prevPage = React.useCallback(() =&gt; goToPage(currentPage - 1), [currentPage, goToPage]);

  const pageNumbers = React.useMemo(() =&gt; {
    const range = (start, end) =&gt; Array.from({ length: end - start + 1 }, (_, i) =&gt; start + i);
    if (totalPages &lt;= 7) return range(1, totalPages);
    if (currentPage &lt;= 4) return [...range(1, 5), '...', totalPages];
    if (currentPage &gt;= totalPages - 3) return [1, '...', ...range(totalPages - 4, totalPages)];
    return [1, '...', ...range(currentPage - 1, currentPage + 1), '...', totalPages];
  }, [currentPage, totalPages]);

  return {
    currentPage, totalPages, pageNumbers,
    nextPage, prevPage, goToPage,
    canGoNext: currentPage &lt; totalPages,
    canGoPrev: currentPage &gt; 1,
    startItem: (currentPage - 1) * pageSize + 1,
    endItem: Math.min(currentPage * pageSize, totalItems),
  };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q75">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement undo/redo with hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q75" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Undo/redo requires maintaining a history stack of past states and a pointer (index) to the current position in that stack. <code>useReducer</code> is the natural fit because all state transitions are explicit typed actions — SET (add to history), UNDO (move index back), and REDO (move index forward). The SET action truncates any redo history beyond the current index before appending the new state, which is the standard undo/redo behavior: making a new change after undoing discards the redo branch. The <code>canUndo</code> and <code>canRedo</code> derived flags allow UI controls to be properly disabled at the boundaries of the history stack.
      <pre class="code-block">function useUndoRedo(initialState) {
  const [history, dispatch] = React.useReducer(
    (state, action) =&gt; {
      switch (action.type) {
        case 'SET': {
          const newHistory = state.history.slice(0, state.index + 1);
          return { history: [...newHistory, action.value], index: state.index + 1 };
        }
        case 'UNDO': return state.index &gt; 0 ? { ...state, index: state.index - 1 } : state;
        case 'REDO': return state.index &lt; state.history.length - 1 ? { ...state, index: state.index + 1 } : state;
        default: return state;
      }
    },
    { history: [initialState], index: 0 }
  );

  return {
    state: history.history[history.index],
    set: (value) =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'SET', value }),
    undo: () =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'UNDO' }),
    redo: () =&gt; dispatch({ type: 'REDO' }),
    canUndo: history.index &gt; 0,
    canRedo: history.index &lt; history.history.length - 1,
  };
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q76">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a useCounter hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q76" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A <code>useCounter</code> hook provides increment, decrement, reset, and set operations with optional min/max/step constraints, making it a clean example of the custom hook pattern where state and the operations that act on it are co-located. The initial value is clamped to the valid min/max range in the state initializer to ensure the hook starts in a consistent state regardless of what the caller passes. All mutation callbacks use the functional updater form of <code>setState</code> to access the latest count without adding it to dependency arrays, and all are wrapped in <code>useCallback</code> for stable references. The <code>set</code> callback clamps its input to the valid range, providing a safe external API.
      <pre class="code-block">function useCounter(initialValue = 0, { min = -Infinity, max = Infinity, step = 1 } = {}) {
  const [count, setCount] = React.useState(
    () =&gt; Math.max(min, Math.min(max, initialValue))
  );

  const increment = React.useCallback(() =&gt;
    setCount(c =&gt; Math.min(c + step, max)), [step, max]);

  const decrement = React.useCallback(() =&gt;
    setCount(c =&gt; Math.max(c - step, min)), [step, min]);

  const reset = React.useCallback(() =&gt; setCount(initialValue), [initialValue]);

  const set = React.useCallback((value) =&gt;
    setCount(Math.max(min, Math.min(max, value))), [min, max]);

  return { count, increment, decrement, reset, set };
}

// Usage
function QuantitySelector({ maxQty }) {
  const { count, increment, decrement } = useCounter(1, { min: 1, max: maxQty });
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={decrement} disabled={count === 1}&gt;-&lt;/button&gt;
      &lt;span&gt;{count}&lt;/span&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={increment} disabled={count === maxQty}&gt;+&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q77">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the React Compiler and how does it relate to hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q77" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The React Compiler (formerly "React Forget") is a build-time Babel plugin that automatically inserts memoization into React components and hooks, eliminating the need for developers to manually write <code>useMemo</code>, <code>useCallback</code>, and <code>React.memo</code>. It performs static analysis of component code to determine the optimal granularity and placement of memoization — something that is notoriously difficult for humans to get right consistently. If the compiler works correctly, developers write clean, simple code as if there were no performance concerns, and the compiler adds the memoization layer automatically at build time. The compiler has a hard requirement that all components and hooks follow the Rules of React — pure render functions with no mutations of props or state, correct hook usage — because it cannot safely optimize impure code. React 19 ships the React Compiler as stable, enabled via <code>babel-plugin-react-compiler</code>, with an accompanying ESLint plugin (<code>eslint-plugin-react-compiler</code>) that flags code patterns the compiler cannot optimize.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q78">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a data-fetching hook that integrates with Suspense?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q78" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      For a component to trigger a Suspense boundary, it must throw a Promise while loading — Suspense catches the thrown Promise, shows the fallback, and retries the component when the Promise resolves. This means the fetching mechanism must cache both the promise and its resolved result so that on re-render the component either throws the same pending promise again, throws the error, or returns the cached data. This "fetch-then-throw" pattern (sometimes called the render-as-you-fetch pattern) requires careful implementation of the cache to avoid re-fetching on every render. React 19's <code>use()</code> hook handles this automatically for any Promise. For React 18, libraries like React Query and SWR provide battle-tested Suspense integration via their <code>suspense: true</code> configuration option, which is strongly preferred over implementing the cache mechanism manually.
      <pre class="code-block">// Suspense-compatible cache (the "fetch-then-throw" pattern)
function createResource(promise) {
  let status = 'pending';
  let result;
  const suspender = promise.then(
    (data) =&gt; { status = 'success'; result = data; },
    (error) =&gt; { status = 'error'; result = error; }
  );
  return {
    read() {
      if (status === 'pending') throw suspender; // Suspense catches this
      if (status === 'error') throw result;      // ErrorBoundary catches this
      return result;                             // success: return data
    }
  };
}

// React Query with Suspense (recommended approach)
function UserProfile({ userId }) {
  // React Query handles the suspend/resolve lifecycle
  const { data: user } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['user', userId],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetchUser(userId),
    suspense: true, // throw promise while loading
  });
  // by here, user is definitely defined
  return &lt;div&gt;{user.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q79">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle component teardown and cleanup for a component that manages multiple resources?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q79" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A component managing multiple resources — WebSocket, polling interval, fetch abort controller, event listeners — needs every resource cleaned up when the component unmounts or when a key dependency changes. The safest pattern is to set up all related resources within a single <code>useEffect</code> and tear them all down in its cleanup function, treating them as an atomic group. Alternatively, each resource can live in its own <code>useEffect</code> with its own cleanup, which is more maintainable when each resource has a different dependency key. Always use an <code>AbortController</code> to cancel in-flight fetch requests on cleanup — attempting to update state after unmount generates React warnings and can cause subtle bugs. Using a cleanup function's local closure to flag whether the effect is still active is the other common pattern for preventing stale state updates.
      <pre class="code-block">function DataDashboard({ dashboardId, userId }) {
  const [metrics, setMetrics] = React.useState(null);
  const [alerts, setAlerts] = React.useState([]);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    // Setup all resources
    const controller = new AbortController();
    const ws = new WebSocket(`wss://api.example.com/dashboard/${dashboardId}`);
    const pollInterval = setInterval(() =&gt; {
      fetch(`/api/metrics/${dashboardId}`, { signal: controller.signal })
        .then(r =&gt; r.json())
        .then(setMetrics)
        .catch(() =&gt; {}); // AbortError on cleanup
    }, 5000);

    ws.onmessage = (e) =&gt; setAlerts(prev =&gt; [...prev, JSON.parse(e.data)]);

    // Clean up ALL resources in one cleanup function
    return () =&gt; {
      controller.abort();           // cancel in-flight fetch
      clearInterval(pollInterval);  // stop polling
      ws.close();                   // close WebSocket
    };
  }, [dashboardId]); // re-run when dashboardId changes

  return &lt;div&gt;{/* render metrics and alerts */}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#hooks-q80">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How would you refactor a large component that has too much logic into smaller composable hooks?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="hooks-q80" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-hooks">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Large components with mixed concerns — data fetching, data transformations, UI interaction state, side effects, and derived values — should be refactored by identifying each distinct responsibility and extracting the state and effects for each into a focused custom hook. The process: (1) identify the distinct responsibilities in the component (product data, reviews, cart state, wishlist state, related products); (2) extract each concern into a hook named after its domain; (3) give each hook a single, clear responsibility; (4) compose the hooks in the component, which becomes a thin orchestration layer that passes data and callbacks to presentational components. The result is that each hook is independently testable, reusable across other components in the application, easy to reason about in isolation, and the component itself shrinks from 150+ lines to 15 lines. This is the "separation of concerns" principle applied to the hooks model.
      <pre class="code-block">// Before: 150-line monolithic component
function ProductPage({ productId }) {
  const [product, setProduct] = React.useState(null);
  const [loading, setLoading] = React.useState(true);
  const [reviews, setReviews] = React.useState([]);
  const [inCart, setInCart] = React.useState(false);
  const [wishlist, setWishlist] = React.useState([]);
  // ... 100+ more lines of mixed concerns
}

// After: composed from focused hooks
function ProductPageRefactored({ productId }) {
  const { product, loading, error } = useProduct(productId);
  const { reviews, addReview } = useProductReviews(productId);
  const { inCart, addToCart, removeFromCart } = useCart(productId);
  const { isWishlisted, toggleWishlist } = useWishlist(productId);
  const { relatedProducts } = useRelatedProducts(productId, product?.categoryId);

  if (loading) return &lt;ProductSkeleton /&gt;;
  if (error) return &lt;ErrorPage error={error} /&gt;;

  return (
    &lt;ProductLayout
      product={product}
      reviews={reviews}
      onAddReview={addReview}
      cart={{ inCart, addToCart, removeFromCart }}
      wishlist={{ isWishlisted, toggleWishlist }}
      relatedProducts={relatedProducts}
    /&gt;
  );
  // Component is now 15 lines -- all logic lives in the hooks
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

  </div>
</div>
<div class="tab-pane fade" id="tab-state" role="tabpanel">
  <div class="accordion" id="acc-state">

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q1">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux is a predictable state container for JavaScript applications, most commonly used with React. It centralizes all application state in a single JavaScript object called the store, making state changes traceable and debuggable. Redux follows three core principles: a single source of truth (one store), state is read-only (only actions can trigger changes), and changes are made with pure functions (reducers). The Redux DevTools extension enables powerful debugging features including time-travel (replaying past states). Redux was created by Dan Abramov and Andrew Clark in 2015, inspired by the Flux architecture and Elm's functional state management.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q2">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are the three principles of Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The three fundamental principles of Redux are: 1) Single Source of Truth — the entire application state is stored in a single JavaScript object tree within one store, making it easy to inspect and debug the full application state at any point. 2) State is Read-Only — the only way to change state is to dispatch an action (a plain object describing what happened); you cannot directly mutate the state. 3) Changes are Made with Pure Functions — to specify how the state tree is transformed by actions, you write pure reducer functions that take the previous state and an action, and return the next state deterministically. These principles together make state changes explicit, predictable, and reversible.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q3">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is an action in Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      An action in Redux is a plain JavaScript object that describes an intent to change the state. Every action must have a <code>type</code> property — a string constant that identifies the kind of change being made (e.g., <code>'ADD_TODO'</code>, <code>'INCREMENT_COUNTER'</code>). Actions can optionally carry additional data in a <code>payload</code> property (the Flux Standard Action convention) that provides the information needed to make the state change. Actions are the only way to get data into the Redux store — you dispatch actions to the store, and the reducers process them. Action creators are functions that return action objects, making it easy to create actions programmatically and keeping the action structure consistent across your codebase.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q4">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is a reducer in Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A reducer is a pure function that takes the current state and an action as arguments, and returns the next state. Reducers must be pure: given the same inputs they always produce the same output, and they produce no side effects — no API calls, no mutations of the input state, no random values. When an action is dispatched, Redux passes the current state and the action to the root reducer, which typically delegates to smaller "slice" reducers responsible for portions of the state tree. The convention is to return the previous state unchanged for unknown action types (<code>default: return state</code>). Reducers must treat state as immutable — they must return new objects/arrays rather than mutating existing ones.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q5">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the Redux store?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The Redux store is the central object that holds the application's state tree, provides methods to dispatch actions and subscribe to state changes, and manages the root reducer. You create a store with <code>configureStore</code> (Redux Toolkit) or the legacy <code>createStore</code>. The store exposes three key methods: <code>getState()</code> (returns the current state), <code>dispatch(action)</code> (sends an action through the reducer and updates state), and <code>subscribe(listener)</code> (registers a callback called after every state change). There is only one store in a Redux application (single source of truth). In React apps, the store is provided to the component tree via the <code>&lt;Provider store={store}&gt;</code> component from <code>react-redux</code>.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q6">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Redux Toolkit and why was it created?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux Toolkit (RTK) is the official, opinionated toolset for Redux development that solves the major pain points of vanilla Redux. The problems RTK solves: too much boilerplate (separate action type constants, action creators, and reducers for every feature), easy to accidentally mutate state (RTK uses Immer under the hood so you can write "mutating" code safely), configuring middleware and DevTools was complex, and writing normalized state and async logic required multiple additional packages. RTK provides <code>configureStore</code> (sets up store with sensible defaults), <code>createSlice</code> (generates action creators and reducers together), <code>createAsyncThunk</code> (handles async logic), and <code>createEntityAdapter</code> (normalized state management). The Redux team officially recommends RTK for all new Redux projects.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q7">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is createSlice in Redux Toolkit?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>createSlice</code> is a function from Redux Toolkit that generates a Redux slice — a combination of a reducer function, action creators, and action types — all from a single configuration object. You provide a <code>name</code> (used to namespace actions), <code>initialState</code>, and a <code>reducers</code> object where each key becomes both a reducer case and an action creator. RTK's Immer integration means reducer functions inside <code>createSlice</code> can use "mutating" syntax (<code>state.value++</code>) and Immer will produce an immutable update under the hood. The returned slice object has <code>.reducer</code> (the generated reducer function to pass to <code>configureStore</code>) and <code>.actions</code> (the generated action creators).
      <pre class="code-block">import { createSlice } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';

const counterSlice = createSlice({
  name: 'counter',
  initialState: { value: 0, status: 'idle' },
  reducers: {
    increment: (state) =&gt; { state.value += 1; },   // Immer: looks like mutation, produces immutable
    decrement: (state) =&gt; { state.value -= 1; },
    incrementByAmount: (state, action) =&gt; { state.value += action.payload; },
  },
});

export const { increment, decrement, incrementByAmount } = counterSlice.actions;
export default counterSlice.reducer;</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q8">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is createAsyncThunk?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>createAsyncThunk</code> is a Redux Toolkit utility for creating async action creators that automatically dispatch <code>pending</code>, <code>fulfilled</code>, and <code>rejected</code> action types based on the lifecycle of a Promise. You provide an action type string prefix and a "payload creator" — an async function that returns a Promise. RTK dispatches <code>prefix/pending</code> when the thunk starts, <code>prefix/fulfilled</code> when the Promise resolves (with the resolved value as payload), and <code>prefix/rejected</code> when it rejects (with the error as payload). You handle these lifecycle actions in <code>extraReducers</code> inside <code>createSlice</code> using the <code>builder.addCase</code> API. This eliminates the boilerplate of manually dispatching multiple actions for async operations.
      <pre class="code-block">import { createAsyncThunk, createSlice } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';

export const fetchUser = createAsyncThunk('user/fetch', async (userId) =&gt; {
  const response = await fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`);
  return response.json();
});

const userSlice = createSlice({
  name: 'user',
  initialState: { data: null, loading: false, error: null },
  reducers: {},
  extraReducers: (builder) =&gt; {
    builder
      .addCase(fetchUser.pending, (state) =&gt; { state.loading = true; })
      .addCase(fetchUser.fulfilled, (state, action) =&gt; {
        state.loading = false;
        state.data = action.payload;
      })
      .addCase(fetchUser.rejected, (state, action) =&gt; {
        state.loading = false;
        state.error = action.error.message;
      });
  },
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q9">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between Redux and the Context API?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux and React Context both solve the problem of sharing data across components without prop drilling, but they are architecturally different. Context is a built-in React feature that provides a way to broadcast a value to all descendant consumers; it has no built-in mechanism for structured state updates, middleware, selectors, or DevTools integration. Redux is a dedicated state management library with a strict unidirectional data flow pattern: actions → reducers → new state → re-render. Redux has a rich middleware system (for async, logging, analytics), the Redux DevTools with time-travel debugging, and selectors (reselect) for derived state memoization. Context re-renders all consumers when the provided value changes; Redux (via react-redux's <code>useSelector</code>) only re-renders components when the selected slice of state changes. For complex, frequently-updated global state, Redux's optimized subscriptions outperform Context.
      <pre class="code-block">// Context: re-renders all consumers on any value change
const GlobalContext = React.createContext(null);
function Provider({ children }) {
  const [state, setState] = React.useState({ user: null, theme: 'light', count: 0 });
  return &lt;GlobalContext.Provider value={{ state, setState }}&gt;{children}&lt;/GlobalContext.Provider&gt;;
}
// Any change to state re-renders ALL consumers — even if they only use `theme`

// Redux: component only re-renders when selected slice changes
function ThemeToggle() {
  const theme = useSelector(state =&gt; state.ui.theme); // only re-renders on theme change
  return &lt;button&gt;{theme}&lt;/button&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q10">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;When should you use Redux vs Context?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Use Context for: low-frequency updates (theme, language, auth user, feature flags) that many components need but that rarely change; small-to-medium apps where the overhead of Redux isn't justified; data that's truly global but doesn't need complex update logic. Use Redux for: complex state with many types of updates and interactions; data that updates frequently and is consumed by many components (performance matters); when you need middleware for side effects (API calls, analytics, WebSockets); when time-travel debugging and action logging are important for your team; when you have complex async workflows; large teams where a strict update pattern reduces bugs. A rough heuristic: if you find yourself writing complex useEffect chains to synchronize Context state, Redux would likely be cleaner. The two are not mutually exclusive — many apps use Redux for server/feature state and Context for static configuration like themes.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q11">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Immer and how does Redux Toolkit use it?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Immer is a JavaScript library that allows you to write "mutating" code that actually produces immutable updates. It uses JavaScript Proxies to intercept mutations on a draft object, recording every change, and then producing a new immutable state based on those recorded mutations. Redux Toolkit wraps all <code>createSlice</code> reducer functions with Immer automatically, so you can write <code>state.user.name = 'Alice'</code> inside a reducer and get an immutable update without manually spreading every level of nesting. This dramatically reduces the verbosity of writing reducers for deeply nested state. Immer also detects if you accidentally return a new value AND mutate the draft, throwing an error to prevent bugs. You can use Immer independently of Redux Toolkit via the <code>produce</code> function.
      <pre class="code-block">// Without Immer (verbose, error-prone)
function reducer(state, action) {
  return {
    ...state,
    users: {
      ...state.users,
      [action.userId]: {
        ...state.users[action.userId],
        profile: {
          ...state.users[action.userId].profile,
          name: action.name,
        }
      }
    }
  };
}

// With Immer (via createSlice) — same result, much simpler
updateUserName: (state, action) =&gt; {
  state.users[action.payload.userId].profile.name = action.payload.name;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q12">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is a selector in Redux? What is reselect?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      A selector is a function that derives data from the Redux store state. The simplest selectors just read a property: <code>state =&gt; state.users.list</code>. More complex selectors compute derived data: filtering, sorting, combining multiple parts of state. Reselect is a library (bundled with RTK) that creates memoized selectors using <code>createSelector</code>. A memoized selector only recomputes when its input selectors produce new values (using reference equality), caching the result otherwise. This prevents unnecessary re-renders in components using <code>useSelector</code> — if a complex derived value (like a filtered, sorted list) would produce the same output, reselect returns the cached result with the same reference, and <code>useSelector</code> skips the re-render. RTK exports <code>createSelector</code> directly from <code>@reduxjs/toolkit</code>.
      <pre class="code-block">import { createSelector } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';

// Input selectors (simple, cheap)
const selectAllTodos = (state) =&gt; state.todos.items;
const selectFilter = (state) =&gt; state.todos.filter;

// Memoized derived selector — only recomputes when todos or filter changes
export const selectFilteredTodos = createSelector(
  [selectAllTodos, selectFilter],
  (todos, filter) =&gt; {
    if (filter === 'all') return todos;
    return todos.filter(t =&gt; t.status === filter);
  }
);

// Component: only re-renders when filtered result changes
function TodoList() {
  const todos = useSelector(selectFilteredTodos);
  return &lt;ul&gt;{todos.map(t =&gt; &lt;li key={t.id}&gt;{t.text}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q13">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is normalized state and why is it important?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Normalized state means storing entities (users, posts, comments) as flat lookup tables (objects keyed by ID) instead of nested arrays of objects. For example, instead of <code>[{ id: 1, name: 'Alice', posts: [{ id: 10, title: '...' }] }]</code>, you store <code>{ users: { 1: { id: 1, name: 'Alice', postIds: [10] } }, posts: { 10: { id: 10, title: '...' } } }</code>. This avoids duplication (the same user appearing in multiple places), makes updates O(1) by ID (no array search), prevents complex cascade updates when an entity changes, and mirrors how a database organizes data. Redux Toolkit's <code>createEntityAdapter</code> provides normalized state utilities out of the box. The normalized shape (inspired by <code>normalizr</code>) is the recommended pattern for any Redux store containing relational data.
      <pre class="code-block">// Normalized state shape
{
  users: {
    ids: [1, 2, 3],
    entities: {
      1: { id: 1, name: 'Alice' },
      2: { id: 2, name: 'Bob' },
    }
  },
  posts: {
    ids: [10, 11],
    entities: {
      10: { id: 10, title: 'Hello', authorId: 1 },
    }
  }
}

// O(1) update — no need to find item in array
state.users.entities[userId].name = newName;</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q14">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is RTK Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q14" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      RTK Query is a powerful data fetching and caching solution built into Redux Toolkit. It eliminates the boilerplate of writing thunks, reducers, and loading/error state for every API endpoint. You define an API using <code>createApi</code> with a <code>baseQuery</code> (typically <code>fetchBaseQuery</code> wrapping fetch) and a set of <code>endpoints</code> (queries for GET-like operations, mutations for POST/PUT/DELETE). RTK Query automatically generates React hooks (e.g., <code>useGetUsersQuery</code>, <code>useCreateUserMutation</code>) that handle caching, loading states, background refetching, invalidation, and optimistic updates. Data is stored in Redux and can be inspected with DevTools. RTK Query reduces a typical CRUD feature from ~200 lines of Redux code to ~30 lines.
      <pre class="code-block">import { createApi, fetchBaseQuery } from '@reduxjs/toolkit/query/react';

const api = createApi({
  reducerPath: 'api',
  baseQuery: fetchBaseQuery({ baseUrl: '/api' }),
  tagTypes: ['User'],
  endpoints: (builder) =&gt; ({
    getUsers: builder.query({ query: () =&gt; '/users', providesTags: ['User'] }),
    createUser: builder.mutation({
      query: (user) =&gt; ({ url: '/users', method: 'POST', body: user }),
      invalidatesTags: ['User'], // automatically refetch users after creation
    }),
  }),
});

export const { useGetUsersQuery, useCreateUserMutation } = api;

// Component
function UserList() {
  const { data, isLoading } = useGetUsersQuery();
  if (isLoading) return &lt;Spinner /&gt;;
  return &lt;ul&gt;{data.map(u =&gt; &lt;li key={u.id}&gt;{u.name}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q15">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between RTK Query and React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Both RTK Query and React Query (TanStack Query) are data-fetching libraries that solve caching, loading states, and synchronization, but they differ in integration and scope. RTK Query is built into Redux Toolkit and stores its cache in Redux — state is inspectable via Redux DevTools, and you can combine server state with Redux client state seamlessly; it is ideal if you're already using Redux. React Query is framework-agnostic (works without Redux), has its own cache independent of Redux, and has a larger ecosystem, more advanced features (infinite queries, optimistic updates, SSR hydration), and better TypeScript ergonomics. React Query is often preferred in non-Redux apps or when a standalone data-fetching solution is preferred. RTK Query wins when deep Redux integration is valuable. Both significantly outperform manual useEffect fetching patterns.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q16">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Zustand?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Zustand is a small, fast, and flexible state management library for React that uses hooks as its primary API. You define a store using <code>create</code>, providing a setup function that receives <code>set</code> and <code>get</code> helpers and returns an object with state and actions. Components subscribe to the store via the generated hook, optionally passing a selector to subscribe to only a portion of the state — preventing re-renders when unrelated state changes. Zustand requires no Provider wrapper, works outside React components (accessing state from utility functions), and supports middleware for persistence, DevTools integration, and Immer-style mutations. Its tiny bundle size (~1KB) and minimal boilerplate make it popular for small-to-medium apps that need global state without Redux's complexity. The lack of enforced patterns is both a feature (flexibility) and a risk (inconsistency in larger teams).
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q17">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Jotai?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Jotai is an atomic state management library for React inspired by Recoil and the Atom model. State is broken into small, independent atoms (think: individual useState values at the global level), and components subscribe to only the atoms they need. Atoms can be composed: derived atoms compute their value from other atoms, similar to reselect selectors but with automatic dependency tracking. Jotai is extremely lightweight, requires no Provider (it uses a default global store), and avoids the context re-render problem since components only re-render when their specific atoms change. It works well for fine-grained, per-entity state (e.g., per-row selected state in a table). The atom model encourages splitting state into small, focused units rather than one large state tree, making incremental adoption alongside existing solutions very easy.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q18">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is Recoil?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Recoil is Facebook's experimental atomic state management library for React. Like Jotai, it uses atoms (units of state) and selectors (derived state functions that auto-track atom dependencies). Components use <code>useRecoilState</code> (like useState but global), <code>useRecoilValue</code> (read-only), and <code>useSetRecoilState</code> (write-only). Recoil's key differentiator is its support for asynchronous selectors — selectors can return Promises, integrating with Suspense for data loading. It also has advanced features like atom families (parameterized atoms), snapshots (capturing full state for debugging/undo), and transactions. Recoil is still in experimental status (as of 2024) and less production-battle-tested than Redux or Zustand, but it demonstrates interesting ideas about fine-grained reactive state that influenced libraries like Jotai.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q19">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the Flux architecture?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Flux is the application architecture pattern that Redux is based on, developed by Facebook as an alternative to MVC for client-side web applications. The core Flux pattern is unidirectional data flow: Action → Dispatcher → Store → View → (user interaction creates) → Action. In Flux, the Dispatcher is a central hub that receives all actions and broadcasts them to every registered store. Stores contain application state and logic and update themselves in response to dispatched actions. Views (React components) render based on store data and dispatch actions on user interaction. Redux simplified Flux by replacing the Dispatcher with pure reducer functions and the multiple stores with a single store. Flux ensures data flows in one direction, making state changes predictable and easier to debug compared to bidirectional data binding in MVC frameworks.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q20">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is middleware in Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux middleware is code that sits between the dispatching of an action and the moment it reaches the reducer. Middleware can intercept actions, modify them, dispatch additional actions, perform async operations, or cancel them entirely. The middleware API is a chain of higher-order functions: <code>store =&gt; next =&gt; action =&gt; { ... next(action) ... }</code>. Common middleware: <code>redux-thunk</code> (allows dispatching functions for async logic), <code>redux-saga</code> (handles complex async with generators), <code>redux-logger</code> (logs every action and state change to the console). Redux Toolkit includes <code>redux-thunk</code> by default via <code>configureStore</code>. Middleware is applied using <code>applyMiddleware</code> (legacy) or the <code>middleware</code> option in <code>configureStore</code>.
      <pre class="code-block">// Custom middleware example: logger
const loggerMiddleware = (store) =&gt; (next) =&gt; (action) =&gt; {
  console.log('Dispatching:', action);
  console.log('State before:', store.getState());
  const result = next(action); // pass to next middleware / reducer
  console.log('State after:', store.getState());
  return result;
};

// Apply middleware
import { configureStore } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';
const store = configureStore({
  reducer: rootReducer,
  middleware: (getDefaultMiddleware) =&gt;
    getDefaultMiddleware().concat(loggerMiddleware),
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q21">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is redux-thunk?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q21" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux-thunk is a middleware that allows you to write action creators that return functions instead of plain action objects. The returned function receives <code>dispatch</code> and <code>getState</code> as arguments, allowing it to dispatch multiple actions and perform async operations (API calls, timers) before dispatching the final action. Without thunk, action creators must return plain objects — you couldn't perform any async logic in them. Thunks are the simplest async middleware and are included by default in Redux Toolkit's <code>configureStore</code>. The name "thunk" comes from computer science: a function that wraps an expression to delay its evaluation. <code>createAsyncThunk</code> in RTK builds on top of redux-thunk to provide the lifecycle action pattern automatically.
      <pre class="code-block">// Manual thunk
export const fetchUserThunk = (userId) =&gt; async (dispatch, getState) =&gt; {
  dispatch({ type: 'user/fetchPending' });
  try {
    const response = await fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`);
    const data = await response.json();
    dispatch({ type: 'user/fetchFulfilled', payload: data });
  } catch (error) {
    dispatch({ type: 'user/fetchRejected', payload: error.message });
  }
};

// RTK's createAsyncThunk does this automatically
export const fetchUser = createAsyncThunk('user/fetch', (userId) =&gt;
  fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`).then(r =&gt; r.json())
);</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q22">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is redux-saga vs redux-thunk?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q22" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Both redux-saga and redux-thunk handle async side effects in Redux, but with very different models. Redux-thunk uses plain async/await functions — simple, widely understood, good for straightforward async operations. Redux-saga uses ES6 generator functions (<code>function*</code>) that <code>yield</code> "effect descriptors" — plain objects describing what to do (call an API, dispatch an action, take an action) — that the saga middleware executes. This makes sagas highly testable (you test the yielded descriptors, not actual API calls), powerful (can cancel tasks, handle complex race conditions with <code>race()</code>, debounce with <code>takeLatest</code>), and composable. The trade-off: sagas have a steep learning curve (generators, saga effects). Use thunks for simple async; use sagas for complex orchestration (wizards, polling, WebSocket management, task cancellation).
      <pre class="code-block">// redux-saga — generator functions, highly testable
import { call, put, takeEvery } from 'redux-saga/effects';

function* fetchUserSaga(action) {
  try {
    const user = yield call(fetchUserAPI, action.payload); // testable descriptor
    yield put({ type: 'user/fetchFulfilled', payload: user });
  } catch (error) {
    yield put({ type: 'user/fetchRejected', error });
  }
}

function* rootSaga() {
  yield takeEvery('user/fetchRequested', fetchUserSaga);
  // takeLatest: cancels previous if new action arrives
  // takeLeading: ignores new actions while one is in progress
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q23">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How does Redux DevTools time travel work?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q23" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux DevTools time travel works because Redux enforces immutable state and pure reducers — every state in the application's history is a separate JavaScript object that can be stored and replayed. The DevTools extension maintains an array of all dispatched actions and all state snapshots. To "travel back in time", it replays all actions from the initial state up to the selected point by re-running the reducer for each action in sequence, producing the exact state at that moment. The component tree then re-renders with that historical state. This is only possible because Redux's reducers are pure functions and state is never mutated — if state were mutable, previous snapshots would be overwritten. The DevTools also support "skip" (remove an action from the log and replay without it), "commit" (set current state as new initial), and "import/export state" features.
      <pre class="code-block">// DevTools is enabled automatically with RTK's configureStore
import { configureStore } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';

const store = configureStore({
  reducer: rootReducer,
  // Redux DevTools are enabled in dev by default
  devTools: process.env.NODE_ENV !== 'production'
    ? { maxAge: 50, actionCreators: { fetchUser, createPost } }
    : false,
});

// How time travel works internally (simplified):
// history = [
//   { action: { type: '@@INIT' }, state: initialState },
//   { action: { type: 'counter/increment' }, state: { count: 1 } },
//   { action: { type: 'counter/increment' }, state: { count: 2 } },
// ]
// Jump to index 1: render with state { count: 1 }</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q24">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the problem with large Redux stores and how do you optimize them?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q24" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Large Redux stores face several problems: any state change causes all <code>useSelector</code> hooks to run their selector functions (even those for unrelated state), though RTK's default equality check usually catches this; large, unnormalized state means updates require deep object spreading that is verbose and slow; keeping all state in one store can cause all features to be loaded upfront even if code splitting is used; and serialization for DevTools becomes slow for very large state trees. Optimizations include: using <code>createEntityAdapter</code> for normalized collections, using <code>createSelector</code> (reselect) for memoized derived state, lazy loading feature reducers with Redux Toolkit's code splitting via <code>injectReducer</code>, splitting contexts so unrelated features don't trigger unnecessary selector runs, and avoiding storing non-serializable data (functions, class instances, DOM refs) in Redux.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q25">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle optimistic updates in Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q25" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Optimistic updates in Redux mean immediately updating the store with the expected server result before the async operation completes, then reconciling when the actual response arrives. The pattern: (1) dispatch an optimistic update action that immediately changes the UI state; (2) make the API call; (3) on success, optionally dispatch a confirmation action with server-generated data (like a real ID replacing a temp ID); (4) on failure, dispatch a rollback action that reverts the state to before the optimistic update. RTK Query has built-in <code>onQueryStarted</code> for optimistic updates with automatic rollback. For manual Redux, you need to preserve the pre-update state to enable rollback.
      <pre class="code-block">// RTK Query optimistic update with rollback
const api = createApi({
  endpoints: (builder) =&gt; ({
    updatePost: builder.mutation({
      query: ({ id, ...patch }) =&gt; ({ url: `/posts/${id}`, method: 'PATCH', body: patch }),
      async onQueryStarted({ id, ...patch }, { dispatch, queryFulfilled }) {
        // Optimistically update cache
        const patchResult = dispatch(
          api.util.updateQueryData('getPost', id, (draft) =&gt; {
            Object.assign(draft, patch); // Immer draft mutation
          })
        );
        try {
          await queryFulfilled; // wait for server response
        } catch {
          patchResult.undo(); // rollback on failure
        }
      },
    }),
  }),
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q26">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between local state and global state and when to use each?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q26" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Local state (useState, useReducer within a component) is owned by a specific component and is not accessible outside it. It is appropriate for: UI state that only one component cares about (modal open/closed, input values, tab selection, hover state), state that is tightly coupled to a specific component's logic, and ephemeral state that doesn't need to survive navigation. Global state (Redux, Context, Zustand) is shared across multiple components and is appropriate for: authenticated user data, theme/language preferences, shopping cart contents, data fetched from an API that multiple views need, and any state that would require significant prop drilling to share. A useful heuristic: start with local state and only elevate to global when you find yourself either prop-drilling or duplicating state in multiple places. Over-globalizing state (putting everything in Redux) is a common anti-pattern that hurts performance and maintainability.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q27">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How does Redux Toolkit's createEntityAdapter work?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q27" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>createEntityAdapter</code> generates a set of prebuilt reducers and selectors for managing normalized collections of entities in a Redux slice. You provide an entity type and optionally a <code>selectId</code> function (defaults to <code>entity.id</code>) and a <code>sortComparer</code>. The adapter generates CRUD reducer methods: <code>addOne</code>, <code>addMany</code>, <code>updateOne</code>, <code>updateMany</code>, <code>removeOne</code>, <code>removeMany</code>, <code>upsertOne</code>, <code>setAll</code>. These can be spread into your slice's <code>reducers</code> object. The adapter also generates selectors: <code>selectAll</code>, <code>selectById</code>, <code>selectIds</code>, <code>selectEntities</code>, <code>selectTotal</code> — pre-bound to the slice's state. The state shape is <code>{ ids: [...], entities: { id: entity, ... } }</code> — the normalized structure. This eliminates the boilerplate of writing CRUD operations and normalized selectors from scratch.
      <pre class="code-block">import { createEntityAdapter, createSlice } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';

const usersAdapter = createEntityAdapter();
const initialState = usersAdapter.getInitialState({ loading: false });

const usersSlice = createSlice({
  name: 'users',
  initialState,
  reducers: {
    userAdded: usersAdapter.addOne,
    usersLoaded: usersAdapter.setAll,
    userUpdated: usersAdapter.updateOne,
    userRemoved: usersAdapter.removeOne,
  },
});

// Selectors
export const {
  selectAll: selectAllUsers,
  selectById: selectUserById,
  selectIds: selectUserIds,
} = usersAdapter.getSelectors((state) =&gt; state.users);</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q28">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle pagination with Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q28" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Pagination with Redux involves storing both the paginated data and pagination metadata (current page, total pages, page size, loading state per page) in the store. There are two common approaches: cursor-based (store a <code>nextCursor</code> token and fetch the next page using it) and offset-based (store <code>currentPage</code> and calculate offset = page * pageSize). The state typically includes a lookup of loaded pages to avoid refetching: <code>{ pages: { 1: [id1, id2, ...], 2: [...] }, entities: { id1: {...} }, currentPage: 1, totalPages: 10, loading: false }</code>. RTK Query handles pagination cleanly: use <code>useGetItemsQuery({ page })</code> and RTK Query caches each page separately. For infinite scroll, use <code>useLazyQuery</code> or implement an accumulate-pages pattern.
      <pre class="code-block">const paginatedSlice = createSlice({
  name: 'posts',
  initialState: { entities: {}, ids: [], pagination: { page: 1, total: 0, loading: false } },
  extraReducers: (builder) =&gt; {
    builder
      .addCase(fetchPosts.pending, (state) =&gt; { state.pagination.loading = true; })
      .addCase(fetchPosts.fulfilled, (state, action) =&gt; {
        const { items, total } = action.payload;
        postsAdapter.setAll(state, items);
        state.pagination.total = total;
        state.pagination.loading = false;
      });
  },
});

function PostList() {
  const [page, setPage] = React.useState(1);
  const dispatch = useDispatch();
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; { dispatch(fetchPosts({ page })); }, [page, dispatch]);
  const posts = useSelector(selectAllPosts);
  return &lt;div&gt;{posts.map(p =&gt; &lt;Post key={p.id} {...p} /&gt;)}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q29">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are the performance implications of Context API for global state?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q29" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The fundamental performance problem with Context is that every consumer re-renders when the provided value reference changes — React does not do a deep comparison of the value, just reference equality. If the Provider renders with a new object <code>value={{ user, theme, count }}</code> on every render (which is common since the object literal creates a new reference), every consumer re-renders regardless of which piece of data they actually use. Solutions: (1) memoize the context value with <code>useMemo</code>; (2) split a large context into smaller, more focused contexts (a UserContext, ThemeContext, etc.) so changes to theme don't re-render user-consuming components; (3) use context only for low-frequency updates and a library like Zustand or Redux for high-frequency state. Large apps with frequently updating state in a single Context can have catastrophic performance issues.
      <pre class="code-block">// PROBLEM: new object reference on every render
function App() {
  const [user, setUser] = React.useState(null);
  const [theme, setTheme] = React.useState('light');
  // New object every render → ALL consumers re-render
  return &lt;Ctx.Provider value={{ user, theme }}&gt;...&lt;/Ctx.Provider&gt;;
}

// FIX 1: memoize the value
function App() {
  const [user, setUser] = React.useState(null);
  const [theme, setTheme] = React.useState('light');
  const value = React.useMemo(() =&gt; ({ user, theme }), [user, theme]);
  return &lt;Ctx.Provider value={value}&gt;...&lt;/Ctx.Provider&gt;;
}

// FIX 2: split contexts
// &lt;UserContext.Provider value={user}&gt;
//   &lt;ThemeContext.Provider value={theme}&gt;
//     {children}
//   &lt;/ThemeContext.Provider&gt;
// &lt;/UserContext.Provider&gt;</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q30">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you split a Redux store into feature modules?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q30" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Redux supports splitting the store into feature modules using <code>combineReducers</code> (or Redux Toolkit's automatic combination in <code>configureStore</code>). Each feature owns its slice file (reducer + actions + selectors), which is combined at the root. For code splitting (loading feature reducers only when the feature's route loads), Redux Toolkit supports dynamic reducer injection via a store enhancer or a custom hook that calls <code>store.replaceReducer</code> when a lazy-loaded feature mounts. The recommended pattern: organize each feature as a "duck" module (all Redux code for that feature in one file or directory), export the slice reducer as default, and import all reducers in the root store configuration. This keeps feature code co-located and prevents the root store from growing with feature-specific logic.
      <pre class="code-block">// store.ts
import { configureStore } from '@reduxjs/toolkit';
import authReducer from '../features/auth/authSlice';
import postsReducer from '../features/posts/postsSlice';
import { api } from '../services/api';

export const store = configureStore({
  reducer: {
    auth: authReducer,
    posts: postsReducer,
    [api.reducerPath]: api.reducer,  // RTK Query
  },
  middleware: (getDefault) =&gt; getDefault().concat(api.middleware),
});

// Dynamic injection for code splitting
function injectReducer(store, key, reducer) {
  if (store.asyncReducers[key]) return;
  store.asyncReducers[key] = reducer;
  store.replaceReducer(createRootReducer(store.asyncReducers));
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q31">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the Provider pattern in React state management?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q31" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The Provider pattern involves a component that wraps part of the component tree and makes data or functionality available to all descendants via React Context, without requiring explicit prop passing. The Provider is responsible for managing the state (via useState or useReducer) and exposing it (and any update functions) through a context value. Any component inside the Provider can consume the provided value using <code>useContext</code>. This pattern is used by React-Redux (<code>&lt;Provider store={store}&gt;</code>), React Query (<code>&lt;QueryClientProvider&gt;</code>), React Router (<code>&lt;BrowserRouter&gt;</code>), and theme libraries. The Provider pattern decouples state management from the UI tree — consumers don't need to know how the state is managed, only how to access it via the context hook. It is commonly paired with a custom hook (e.g., <code>useAuth()</code>) that wraps <code>useContext</code> and adds type safety or error checking.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q32">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the atom pattern in Jotai/Recoil?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q32" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The atom pattern models application state as a collection of small, independent state units (atoms) rather than a single large state tree. Each atom represents one piece of state (similar to a global useState), and components subscribe to only the atoms they need, re-rendering only when those specific atoms change. Atoms can be composed: derived atoms (called selectors in Recoil, or computed atoms in Jotai) automatically track their atom dependencies and recompute only when those dependencies change. This fine-grained reactivity means a change to a user's email doesn't cause a component that only reads the user's name to re-render. The atom model is extremely granular and scalable for large apps with many independently updating pieces of state, and it avoids the "one context re-renders everything" problem of React Context. The trade-off is managing a large number of atoms across a codebase requires discipline.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q33">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How does Zustand differ from Redux conceptually?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q33" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Zustand is much simpler and less structured than Redux. Where Redux enforces strict patterns (actions, reducers, immutable updates, dispatch), Zustand is permissive — you mutate state directly inside action functions using Immer (if configured) or just use <code>set</code>. There is no explicit action type system, no dispatch, no separate action creators; you just call functions on the store object. Zustand doesn't require a Provider component — the store is a module-level singleton. Zustand's selective subscription (components only re-render when their selected slice changes) is a first-class feature, whereas Redux requires careful selector memoization for the same result. Zustand is ideal for simple-to-medium global state; Redux scales better for large teams and complex state with extensive async workflows and debugging needs.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q34">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is MobX and how does it differ from Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q34" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      MobX is a reactive state management library that automatically tracks which components observe which state and updates only those components when the observed state changes. Instead of the explicit dispatch → reducer → update cycle of Redux, MobX uses observable state objects and derivations — you mark state as <code>@observable</code>, mark computed values as <code>@computed</code>, and mark UI-updating code as <code>@observer</code>. When observable state changes, MobX automatically re-runs all observer components that read that state. MobX is more "magic" (automatic dependency tracking) versus Redux's explicit data flow. MobX is more flexible (any state mutation triggers updates) while Redux enforces immutability and explicit updates. MobX with React requires fewer lines of code for equivalent functionality but is harder to debug since changes can happen from anywhere without a clear audit trail.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q35">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the observer pattern in state management?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q35" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The observer pattern is a design pattern where subjects (state stores) maintain a list of observers (subscribers/listeners) and automatically notify them when the subject's state changes. In React state management, it underpins many libraries: Redux's <code>store.subscribe()</code> notifies all subscribers on every dispatch; MobX's <code>@observer</code> decorator makes a component an observer of the observables it reads; Zustand's hook system subscribes components to the parts of the store they access. The React component that subscribes to the store is the "observer"; the store is the "subject." This pattern enables decoupled, reactive architectures where state changes automatically propagate to all interested parties without explicit event handling chains. React's own useState and useReducer are local implementations of the same idea — the component subscribes to its own state and re-renders when it changes.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q36">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle async state in Redux Toolkit (pending/fulfilled/rejected)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q36" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      RTK's <code>createAsyncThunk</code> generates three action types for each async operation: <code>prefix/pending</code> (dispatched when the thunk starts), <code>prefix/fulfilled</code> (dispatched with the resolved value on success), and <code>prefix/rejected</code> (dispatched with the error on failure). In <code>createSlice</code>, you handle these in <code>extraReducers</code> using the builder API. The standard pattern is to store <code>status: 'idle' | 'loading' | 'succeeded' | 'failed'</code> and <code>error: null | string</code> alongside the data. This three-state model maps cleanly to the UI: show spinner for loading, show data for succeeded, show error message for failed, and show initial UI for idle. RTK also provides <code>thunkArg</code>, <code>requestId</code>, and <code>requestStatus</code> on the thunk result for advanced abort handling.
      <pre class="code-block">const postsSlice = createSlice({
  name: 'posts',
  initialState: { items: [], status: 'idle', error: null },
  reducers: {},
  extraReducers: (builder) =&gt; {
    builder
      .addCase(fetchPosts.pending, (state) =&gt; { state.status = 'loading'; state.error = null; })
      .addCase(fetchPosts.fulfilled, (state, action) =&gt; {
        state.status = 'succeeded';
        state.items = action.payload;
      })
      .addCase(fetchPosts.rejected, (state, action) =&gt; {
        state.status = 'failed';
        state.error = action.error.message;
      });
  },
});

function Posts() {
  const { items, status, error } = useSelector(s =&gt; s.posts);
  if (status === 'loading') return &lt;Spinner /&gt;;
  if (status === 'failed') return &lt;Error message={error} /&gt;;
  return &lt;ul&gt;{items.map(p =&gt; &lt;li key={p.id}&gt;{p.title}&lt;/li&gt;)}&lt;/ul&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q37">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is optimistic locking in UI state management?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q37" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Optimistic locking in UI state management is a pattern where you allow the user to make changes optimistically (immediately updating the UI without waiting for server confirmation) but detect and handle conflicts when the server rejects the update because the server-side data changed since the client last fetched it. The server uses a version number or ETag — when the client submits an update, it includes the version it last saw; if the server's current version is different, it rejects with a conflict error. The UI must then handle this by showing the user a conflict dialog, allowing them to choose between their changes and the server's current data, or automatically merging if possible. This is more complex than simple optimistic updates because you must handle the case where optimistic updates fail not due to network errors but due to concurrent modifications from other users. It is essential in collaborative editing applications.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q38">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement undo/redo functionality with Redux?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q38" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Undo/redo with Redux leverages immutable state history. The <code>redux-undo</code> library is the standard approach: it wraps a reducer and maintains a history stack of past and future states. Dispatching <code>ActionCreators.undo()</code> moves back one step; <code>ActionCreators.redo()</code> moves forward. Manually, you maintain <code>{ past: [state1, state2, ...], present: currentState, future: [] }</code>. On any normal action, push <code>present</code> to <code>past</code>, set <code>present</code> to the new state, and clear <code>future</code>. On undo, pop from <code>past</code> into <code>present</code> (pushing current <code>present</code> to <code>future</code>). On redo, pop from <code>future</code> into <code>present</code>. Filter which actions are undoable with <code>redux-undo</code>'s <code>filter</code> option — you typically don't want to undo navigation actions or server sync actions.
      <pre class="code-block">// Using redux-undo
import undoable, { ActionCreators } from 'redux-undo';

const undoableCounter = undoable(counterReducer, {
  limit: 20, // max history depth
  filter: (action) =&gt; action.type !== 'sync/serverUpdate', // skip some actions
});

// Dispatch undo/redo
dispatch(ActionCreators.undo());
dispatch(ActionCreators.redo());

// State shape with redux-undo:
// { past: [...], present: currentState, future: [...], index: 2 }</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#state-q39">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;Explain the concept of "derived state" vs "source of truth" state.
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="state-q39" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-state">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Source of truth state is the minimal, canonical set of data that everything else can be computed from. Derived state is any value that can be calculated from source of truth state — it is not stored but computed on demand.
