        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q15" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q15">
          <span class="badge bg-warning text-dark me-2">Medium</span> 15. What is Yup and how does it compare to Zod?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q15" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Yup is a JavaScript schema validation library (predates TypeScript) that defines schemas using a fluent chainable API. It has been the dominant React form validation library for years, especially paired with Formik. Compared to Zod: Yup uses chainable methods (<code>yup.string().email().required()</code>) while Zod uses method chaining on types (<code>z.string().email()</code>); Zod has better TypeScript integration (full type inference from the start) while Yup's TypeScript support was added later and is less seamless; Zod is generally considered the modern standard for new TypeScript projects while Yup remains common in existing Formik-based codebases; both integrate with React Hook Form via <code>@hookform/resolvers</code>. Zod is preferred for new projects; Yup is fine for existing Formik/Yup setups.</p>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q16 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q16">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q16" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q16">
          <span class="badge bg-warning text-dark me-2">Medium</span> 16. How do you handle file uploads in React?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q16" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>File uploads in React use an <code>&lt;input type="file"&gt;</code> which is inherently uncontrolled — you cannot set its value via <code>value</code> prop. The file object is read from <code>event.target.files[0]</code> in an <code>onChange</code> handler and stored in state (as a <code>File</code> object, not a path). To display a preview, create an object URL with <code>URL.createObjectURL(file)</code> and use it as an <code>&lt;img src&gt;</code>. To upload, create a <code>FormData</code> object, append the file, and send via fetch/axios with <code>Content-Type: multipart/form-data</code> (or let the browser set this automatically). Clean up object URLs with <code>URL.revokeObjectURL()</code> to prevent memory leaks. For drag-and-drop, use a library like <code>react-dropzone</code>.</p>
<pre class="code-block">function FileUpload() {
  const [file, setFile] = React.useState(null);
  const [preview, setPreview] = React.useState(null);
  const [uploading, setUploading] = React.useState(false);

  const handleFileChange = (e) =&gt; {
    const selectedFile = e.target.files[0];
    if (!selectedFile) return;
    setFile(selectedFile);
    setPreview(URL.createObjectURL(selectedFile)); // create preview URL
  };

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    return () =&gt; { if (preview) URL.revokeObjectURL(preview); }; // cleanup
  }, [preview]);

  const handleUpload = async () =&gt; {
    if (!file) return;
    setUploading(true);
    const formData = new FormData();
    formData.append('file', file);
    await fetch('/api/upload', { method: 'POST', body: formData });
    setUploading(false);
  };

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;input type="file" accept="image/*" onChange={handleFileChange} /&gt;
      {preview &amp;&amp; &lt;img src={preview} alt="preview" style={{ width: 200 }} /&gt;}
      &lt;button onClick={handleUpload} disabled={!file || uploading}&gt;Upload&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q17 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q17">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q17" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q17">
          <span class="badge bg-warning text-dark me-2">Medium</span> 17. How do you implement a multi-step form?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q17" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>A multi-step form breaks a long form into sequential steps, showing one step at a time. The implementation involves: state to track the current step, a central state object accumulating all field data across steps, conditional rendering to show the current step's fields, "Next" and "Back" navigation that updates the step index, and a final submit on the last step. React Hook Form can be used per-step with <code>getValues()</code> to accumulate data, or with a single form context if preferred. For complex wizards, use <code>useReducer</code> with a state machine (current step + accumulated data). The URL can reflect the current step using query params (<code>?step=2</code>) so users can bookmark or share in-progress forms.</p>
<pre class="code-block">function MultiStepForm() {
  const [step, setStep] = React.useState(1);
  const [formData, setFormData] = React.useState({});
  const methods = useForm({ defaultValues: formData });

  const saveAndNext = (data) =&gt; {
    setFormData(prev =&gt; ({ ...prev, ...data }));
    setStep(s =&gt; s + 1);
  };

  const handleFinalSubmit = (data) =&gt; {
    const allData = { ...formData, ...data };
    submitForm(allData);
  };

  return (
    &lt;FormProvider {...methods}&gt;
      {step === 1 &amp;&amp; &lt;StepOne onNext={methods.handleSubmit(saveAndNext)} /&gt;}
      {step === 2 &amp;&amp; &lt;StepTwo onNext={methods.handleSubmit(saveAndNext)} onBack={() =&gt; setStep(1)} /&gt;}
      {step === 3 &amp;&amp; &lt;StepThree onSubmit={methods.handleSubmit(handleFinalSubmit)} onBack={() =&gt; setStep(2)} /&gt;}
    &lt;/FormProvider&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q18 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q18">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q18" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q18">
          <span class="badge bg-warning text-dark me-2">Medium</span> 18. How do you reset a form in React Hook Form?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q18" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>React Hook Form provides a <code>reset(values?, options?)</code> function that resets the entire form to its default values (or to new provided values). Calling <code>reset()</code> without arguments resets to the <code>defaultValues</code> specified in <code>useForm</code>. Calling <code>reset(newValues)</code> resets to the provided values — useful for pre-filling an edit form with data fetched from an API. The <code>options</code> object can control which parts of <code>formState</code> to reset (<code>keepErrors</code>, <code>keepDirty</code>, <code>keepTouched</code>, etc.). After a successful form submission, calling <code>reset()</code> is the standard pattern to clear the form. For async default values (fetched data), use the <code>useEffect</code> + <code>reset</code> pattern: when the fetched data arrives, call <code>reset(fetchedData)</code> to populate the form.</p>
<pre class="code-block">const { register, handleSubmit, reset, formState } = useForm({
  defaultValues: { name: '', email: '' }
});

// Reset to defaults after submit
const onSubmit = async (data) =&gt; {
  await saveData(data);
  reset(); // back to { name: '', email: '' }
};

// Reset with new values (pre-filling edit form)
React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
  if (user) reset({ name: user.name, email: user.email });
}, [user, reset]);</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q19 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q19">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q19" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q19">
          <span class="badge bg-warning text-dark me-2">Medium</span> 19. What is the Controller component in React Hook Form?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q19" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p><code>Controller</code> is a wrapper component from React Hook Form for integrating controlled third-party UI components that don't expose a native DOM ref (like React Select, Material-UI inputs, date pickers, or custom components). Since <code>register</code> relies on a native DOM ref, it doesn't work with components that manage their own state internally. <code>Controller</code> takes a <code>name</code> (field name), <code>control</code> (from <code>useForm</code>), <code>rules</code> (validation), and a <code>render</code> prop — a function that receives <code>{ field, fieldState }</code> and returns the controlled component. The <code>field</code> object contains <code>{ name, value, onChange, onBlur, ref }</code> that you spread onto the component's props.</p>
<pre class="code-block">import { Controller, useForm } from 'react-hook-form';
import Select from 'react-select';

function MyForm() {
  const { control, handleSubmit } = useForm();

  return (
    &lt;form onSubmit={handleSubmit(console.log)}&gt;
      &lt;Controller
        name="country"
        control={control}
        rules={{ required: 'Country is required' }}
        render={({ field, fieldState }) =&gt; (
          &lt;div&gt;
            &lt;Select
              {...field}
              options={countryOptions}
              placeholder="Select country..."
            /&gt;
            {fieldState.error &amp;&amp; &lt;p&gt;{fieldState.error.message}&lt;/p&gt;}
          &lt;/div&gt;
        )}
      /&gt;
      &lt;button type="submit"&gt;Submit&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q20 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q20">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q20" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q20">
          <span class="badge bg-warning text-dark me-2">Medium</span> 20. What is useFieldArray in React Hook Form?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q20" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p><code>useFieldArray({ control, name })</code> is a hook for managing arrays of fields in a form — like a list of addresses, product line items, or team members that can be added, removed, or reordered dynamically. It returns an <code>{ fields, append, prepend, remove, insert, update, move, swap }</code> API. <code>fields</code> is the array of field objects with stable <code>id</code> values (use as keys in JSX). The array operations handle all the complexity of updating nested array state in RHF's internal model. Each field in the array can have multiple sub-fields. This replaces the complex manual state management pattern of handling arrays of form data.</p>
<pre class="code-block">import { useFieldArray, useForm } from 'react-hook-form';

function TeamForm() {
  const { control, register, handleSubmit } = useForm({
    defaultValues: { members: [{ name: '', role: '' }] }
  });
  const { fields, append, remove } = useFieldArray({ control, name: 'members' });

  return (
    &lt;form onSubmit={handleSubmit(console.log)}&gt;
      {fields.map((field, index) =&gt; (
        &lt;div key={field.id}&gt;
          &lt;input {...register(`members.${index}.name`)} placeholder="Name" /&gt;
          &lt;input {...register(`members.${index}.role`)} placeholder="Role" /&gt;
          &lt;button type="button" onClick={() =&gt; remove(index)}&gt;Remove&lt;/button&gt;
        &lt;/div&gt;
      ))}
      &lt;button type="button" onClick={() =&gt; append({ name: '', role: '' })}&gt;Add Member&lt;/button&gt;
      &lt;button type="submit"&gt;Submit&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q21 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q21">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q21" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q21">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 21. How do you handle async validation (e.g., check if username is taken)?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q21" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q21" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Async validation checks server-side constraints that can't be done client-side — like username uniqueness, email availability, or domain verification. In React Hook Form, the <code>validate</code> option accepts async functions that return a Promise resolving to <code>true</code> or an error string. The challenge is performance: you don't want an API call on every keystroke. Debounce the validation: use <code>mode: 'onBlur'</code> in useForm to only validate when the user leaves the field, or combine with a debounced custom validator. A <code>useRef</code> flag (<code>isChecking</code>) prevents duplicate concurrent requests. Show a "checking..." indicator using <code>formState.isValidating</code>. Rate limit with a debounce inside the validator using a module-level timer variable.</p>
<pre class="code-block">import { useForm } from 'react-hook-form';

let debounceTimer;

function SignupForm() {
  const { register, formState: { errors, isValidating } } = useForm({ mode: 'onChange' });

  const checkUsername = (value) =&gt;
    new Promise((resolve) =&gt; {
      clearTimeout(debounceTimer);
      debounceTimer = setTimeout(async () =&gt; {
        const available = await checkUsernameAPI(value);
        resolve(available ? true : 'Username already taken');
      }, 500); // debounce 500ms
    });

  return (
    &lt;form&gt;
      &lt;input {...register('username', {
        required: 'Required',
        minLength: { value: 3, message: 'Min 3 chars' },
        validate: { available: checkUsername },
      })} /&gt;
      {isValidating &amp;&amp; &lt;span&gt;Checking...&lt;/span&gt;}
      {errors.username &amp;&amp; &lt;p&gt;{errors.username.message}&lt;/p&gt;}
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q22 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q22">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q22" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q22">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 22. How do you implement dependent field validation?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q22" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q22" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Dependent field validation means one field's validity depends on another field's value — "confirm password must match password" being the classic example. In React Hook Form, access other field values inside a <code>validate</code> function using <code>getValues()</code>. For Zod schemas, use <code>.refine()</code> at the object level (after both fields are defined) to add cross-field validation. For Yup, use <code>yup.ref()</code> to reference another field. When the first field changes, you must re-validate the dependent field manually by calling <code>trigger('confirmPassword')</code> in the first field's <code>onChange</code> handler, otherwise the dependent field won't re-validate when the source changes.</p>
<pre class="code-block">// Zod approach — cleanest for multiple cross-field validations
const schema = z.object({
  password: z.string().min(8),
  confirmPassword: z.string(),
  startDate: z.date(),
  endDate: z.date(),
})
.refine(data =&gt; data.password === data.confirmPassword, {
  message: 'Passwords must match',
  path: ['confirmPassword'], // attach error to this field
})
.refine(data =&gt; data.endDate &gt; data.startDate, {
  message: 'End date must be after start date',
  path: ['endDate'],
});

// RHF with getValues approach
const { register, getValues, trigger } = useForm();
&lt;input {...register('password', {
  onChange: () =&gt; trigger('confirmPassword'), // re-validate confirm when password changes
})} /&gt;
&lt;input {...register('confirmPassword', {
  validate: (v) =&gt; v === getValues('password') || 'Passwords must match',
})} /&gt;</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q23 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q23">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q23" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q23">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 23. How do you handle form state with Redux?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q23" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q23" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Handling form state in Redux is generally discouraged — forms benefit from fast local state (every keystroke should not dispatch a Redux action) and Redux state is overkill for form-local concerns. However, there are legitimate cases: multi-page checkout forms where form state must persist across navigation; forms where external Redux state (like loaded entity data) needs to pre-fill fields; and forms where submitted data needs to be in Redux. The recommended approach: use React Hook Form or local state for the interactive form experience, and only dispatch to Redux on successful submission. If pre-filling from Redux is needed, use the <code>reset()</code> function or <code>defaultValues</code> from the Redux store. Avoid the anti-pattern of putting raw form field values in Redux.</p>
<pre class="code-block">// Pattern: form state local, submit to Redux
function EditUserForm({ userId }) {
  const user = useSelector(selectUserById(userId));
  const dispatch = useDispatch();
  const { register, handleSubmit, reset } = useForm();

  // Pre-fill from Redux when data arrives
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (user) reset({ name: user.name, email: user.email });
  }, [user, reset]);

  const onSubmit = async (data) =&gt; {
    // Dispatch to Redux on submit — not on every keystroke
    await dispatch(updateUser({ id: userId, ...data }));
  };

  return (
    &lt;form onSubmit={handleSubmit(onSubmit)}&gt;
      &lt;input {...register('name')} /&gt;
      &lt;input {...register('email')} /&gt;
      &lt;button type="submit"&gt;Save&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q24 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q24">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q24" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q24">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 24. What are the performance considerations for large forms?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q24" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q24" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Large forms (20+ fields) can become slow if using fully controlled inputs because each keystroke triggers a state update and re-render of the entire form. Optimizations: (1) Use React Hook Form (uncontrolled by default) which avoids re-renders during typing and only re-renders when validation errors change; (2) If using controlled inputs, split the form into smaller sub-components with <code>React.memo</code> so only the changed field's component re-renders; (3) Use <code>mode: 'onBlur'</code> in RHF to validate on focus loss rather than on every keystroke; (4) Virtualize long dynamic lists (<code>useFieldArray</code> with 100+ items); (5) Debounce expensive operations like async validation; (6) Use <code>shouldUnregister: false</code> (RHF default) to keep field values when the field unmounts (multi-step forms); (7) Avoid <code>watch()</code> for all fields — use <code>getValues()</code> when you only need values at submit time.</p>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q25 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q25">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q25" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q25">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 25. How do you implement a drag-and-drop file upload?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q25" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q25" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Drag-and-drop file upload requires handling the <code>dragover</code>, <code>dragenter</code>, <code>dragleave</code>, and <code>drop</code> events on a drop zone container. Call <code>event.preventDefault()</code> on <code>dragover</code> to allow dropping. On <code>drop</code>, get the files from <code>event.dataTransfer.files</code>. Display a visual "active" state during drag by tracking dragover state. Libraries like <code>react-dropzone</code> handle all this complexity with accessibility (keyboard support, screen reader announcements) and multiple file support. For progress indication, use XMLHttpRequest instead of fetch (XHR has an <code>upload.onprogress</code> event); or use axios with its <code>onUploadProgress</code> config option.</p>
<pre class="code-block">import { useDropzone } from 'react-dropzone';

function DropZone({ onUpload }) {
  const [uploading, setUploading] = React.useState(false);
  const [progress, setProgress] = React.useState(0);

  const onDrop = React.useCallback(async (acceptedFiles) =&gt; {
    setUploading(true);
    const formData = new FormData();
    acceptedFiles.forEach(file =&gt; formData.append('files', file));
    await axios.post('/api/upload', formData, {
      onUploadProgress: (e) =&gt; setProgress(Math.round((e.loaded * 100) / e.total)),
    });
    setUploading(false);
    onUpload?.();
  }, [onUpload]);

  const { getRootProps, getInputProps, isDragActive, acceptedFiles } = useDropzone({
    onDrop, accept: { 'image/*': [], 'application/pdf': [] }, maxFiles: 5,
  });

  return (
    &lt;div {...getRootProps()} className={isDragActive ? 'dropzone active' : 'dropzone'}&gt;
      &lt;input {...getInputProps()} /&gt;
      {isDragActive ? &lt;p&gt;Drop files here...&lt;/p&gt; : &lt;p&gt;Drag files or click to select&lt;/p&gt;}
      {uploading &amp;&amp; &lt;progress value={progress} max={100} /&gt;}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q26 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q26">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q26" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q26">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 26. How do you preserve form state across route navigation?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q26" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q26" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>By default, navigating away from a route unmounts the form component, losing all form state. To preserve it: (1) Lift form state to a parent component or global state (Context, Redux, Zustand) that persists across navigation — pass the saved data back as <code>defaultValues</code> when remounting; (2) Use React Router's navigation state: on navigate away, save current form values to <code>navigate('/route', { state: { formData } })</code>; (3) Use React Hook Form's <code>shouldUnregister: false</code> — fields preserve their values even when unmounted from a multi-step form; (4) Store in localStorage (<code>useLocalStorage</code> hook) for persistence that survives tab closes; (5) Use Remix's or Next.js's Server Actions which submit to a server, preserving state server-side. The best pattern for a multi-step form: use a parent component's state as the accumulator, passing values into each step as <code>defaultValues</code>.</p>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q27 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q27">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q27" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q27">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 27. How do you implement auto-save functionality?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q27" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q27" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Auto-save saves form data automatically as the user makes changes, without requiring an explicit save action. The implementation: watch form values (RHF's <code>watch()</code> or controlled state), debounce the changes (don't save on every keystroke — wait 1-2 seconds after the user stops), and call the save API when the debounced value updates. Indicate save status with a "Saved" / "Saving..." message. Handle errors gracefully (the user may be offline). Key considerations: debounce carefully — too short = too many API calls; too long = data loss risk. Use an AbortController to cancel the in-flight save request if a new change comes in before the previous save completes.</p>
<pre class="code-block">function AutoSavingEditor({ documentId, initialContent }) {
  const [content, setContent] = React.useState(initialContent);
  const [saveStatus, setSaveStatus] = React.useState('saved'); // 'saved' | 'saving' | 'error'
  const debouncedContent = useDebounce(content, 1500);
  const isFirstRender = React.useRef(true);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (isFirstRender.current) { isFirstRender.current = false; return; } // skip initial render
    if (!debouncedContent) return;
    setSaveStatus('saving');
    saveDocument(documentId, debouncedContent)
      .then(() =&gt; setSaveStatus('saved'))
      .catch(() =&gt; setSaveStatus('error'));
  }, [debouncedContent, documentId]);

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;textarea value={content} onChange={e =&gt; { setContent(e.target.value); setSaveStatus('typing'); }} /&gt;
      &lt;span&gt;{saveStatus === 'saving' ? 'Saving...' : saveStatus === 'error' ? 'Save failed' : 'Saved'}&lt;/span&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q28 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q28">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q28" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q28">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 28. What is the difference between form-level and field-level validation?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q28" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q28" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Field-level validation validates each field independently as it changes, providing immediate feedback per field without requiring the entire form to be valid. It runs the validator for a single field when that field changes or loses focus. Form-level validation validates the entire form object at once — typically at submit time or when any field changes — and can access all field values simultaneously, enabling cross-field validation (e.g., end date &gt; start date, password === confirmPassword). Form-level validation is generally cleaner with schema validators (Zod, Yup) that validate the entire shape at once. React Hook Form supports both: <code>validate</code> in <code>register</code> options is field-level; using a <code>resolver</code> (<code>zodResolver</code>, <code>yupResolver</code>) is form-level. For most production forms, a form-level schema resolver with field-level display of errors is the cleanest pattern.</p>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q29 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q29">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q29" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q29">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 29. How do you handle form accessibility (a11y)?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q29" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q29" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>Accessible forms require: (1) <code>&lt;label&gt;</code> elements associated with each input via <code>htmlFor</code> + <code>id</code> (or wrapping the input); (2) Error messages announced to screen readers via <code>aria-describedby</code> linking the input to its error element; (3) <code>aria-invalid="true"</code> on inputs with errors so screen readers announce the invalid state; (4) Focus management — when validation fails on submit, move focus to the first invalid field; (5) Required fields indicated via <code>aria-required="true"</code> or the <code>required</code> attribute; (6) Clear, plain-language error messages (not just "invalid", but "Email must contain @"); (7) Keyboard navigation — all interactive elements must be reachable via Tab; (8) Avoid relying on color alone to indicate errors — use icons, text, and sufficient contrast. React Hook Form integrates well with aria attributes; use the <code>aria-invalid</code> prop conditionally based on <code>formState.errors</code>.</p>
<pre class="code-block">function AccessibleInput({ name, label, register, error }) {
  const id = `field-${name}`;
  const errorId = `${id}-error`;

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;label htmlFor={id}&gt;{label}&lt;/label&gt;
      &lt;input
        id={id}
        {...register(name)}
        aria-invalid={error ? 'true' : 'false'}
        aria-describedby={error ? errorId : undefined}
        aria-required="true"
      /&gt;
      {error &amp;&amp; (
        &lt;p id={errorId} role="alert" aria-live="polite"&gt;
          {error.message}
        &lt;/p&gt;
      )}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

    <!-- forms-q30 -->
    <div class="accordion-item">
      <h2 class="accordion-header" id="heading-forms-q30">
        <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#forms-q30" aria-expanded="false" aria-controls="forms-q30">
          <span class="badge bg-danger me-2">Hard</span> 30. How do you implement a WYSIWYG editor in React?
        </button>
      </h2>
      <div id="forms-q30" class="accordion-collapse collapse" aria-labelledby="heading-forms-q30" data-bs-parent="#acc-forms">
        <div class="accordion-body">
          <p>WYSIWYG (What You See Is What You Get) editors are complex components that render formatted text in a <code>contentEditable</code> div. In React, you integrate third-party editor libraries since building one from scratch is extremely complex. Popular options: TipTap (headless, based on ProseMirror, excellent React integration with hooks API), Quill (mature, widely used, requires a wrapper component), Slate.js (highly customizable, React-native), Draft.js (Facebook, unmaintained), and TinyMCE/CKEditor (full-featured commercial options). The integration challenge: these editors are controlled differently from standard inputs; use <code>Controller</code> from React Hook Form to wrap them. The editor's value is typically HTML or JSON; handle this by converting between formats for storage and display.</p>
<pre class="code-block">// TipTap integration with React Hook Form
import { useEditor, EditorContent } from '@tiptap/react';
import StarterKit from '@tiptap/starter-kit';
import { Controller } from 'react-hook-form';

function RichTextEditor({ name, control, defaultValue = '' }) {
  return (
    &lt;Controller
      name={name}
      control={control}
      defaultValue={defaultValue}
      render={({ field }) =&gt; {
        const editor = useEditor({
          extensions: [StarterKit],
          content: field.value,
          onUpdate: ({ editor }) =&gt; field.onChange(editor.getHTML()),
        });
        return (
          &lt;div className="editor-wrapper"&gt;
            &lt;MenuBar editor={editor} /&gt;
            &lt;EditorContent editor={editor} /&gt;
          &lt;/div&gt;
        );
      }}
    /&gt;
  );
}</pre>
        </div>
      </div>
    </div>

  </div>
</div>
<div class="tab-pane fade" id="tab-api" role="tabpanel">
  <div class="accordion" id="acc-api">

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q1">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is React Query (TanStack Query)?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q1" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query (now TanStack Query) is a data-fetching and server-state management library for React. It handles all the complexity of fetching, caching, synchronizing, and updating server state. Unlike Redux which manages client state, React Query manages server state — data that lives on the server and needs to be fetched, cached, and kept fresh. Key features: automatic caching with configurable stale times, background refetching, query invalidation, optimistic updates, infinite queries, and request deduplication. It eliminates the need for useEffect-based fetching patterns and dramatically reduces boilerplate.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q2">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between server state and client state?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q2" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Server state is data that lives on the server — it's asynchronous, can become stale, needs to be fetched, and may be modified by other users (product inventory, user profiles, posts). Client state is local UI state that exists only in the browser — synchronous, always up-to-date, owned entirely by the client (modal open/closed, form input values, selected tab). Most state management libraries (Redux, Zustand, Context) are designed for client state. React Query, SWR, and Apollo Client are designed for server state. The mistake of managing server state in Redux leads to complex async middleware, manual cache invalidation, and stale data problems that these dedicated libraries solve automatically.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q3">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the useQuery hook?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q3" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useQuery({ queryKey, queryFn })</code> fetches and caches data from an API. <code>queryKey</code> is an array that uniquely identifies the query — it's used for caching, deduplication, and invalidation. <code>queryFn</code> is an async function that fetches and returns the data. It returns <code>{ data, isLoading, isError, error, refetch, status }</code>. React Query automatically: fetches on mount, caches the result, refetches on window focus (configurable), deduplicates concurrent requests with the same key, and retries failed requests. The query key is like a cache key — changing any element in the array triggers a new fetch.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q4">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is useMutation in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q4" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useMutation({ mutationFn, onSuccess, onError, onSettled })</code> manages write operations — creating, updating, or deleting data. Unlike <code>useQuery</code> (which runs automatically), mutations are triggered manually by calling <code>mutate(variables)</code>. It tracks <code>isLoading</code>, <code>isError</code>, <code>data</code>, and <code>error</code> state. Callbacks: <code>onSuccess(data, variables)</code> runs after success, <code>onError(error, variables)</code> after failure, <code>onSettled</code> after either. The most powerful pattern: <code>onSuccess</code> invalidates related queries (<code>queryClient.invalidateQueries(['posts'])</code>) so the UI reflects the mutation result. This is how React Query keeps server state synchronized after mutations.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q5">
      <span class="diff-easy">Easy</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is SWR?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q5" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      SWR (Stale-While-Revalidate) is Vercel's data-fetching library for React. The name comes from the HTTP cache-control strategy: return cached (stale) data immediately, then revalidate in the background. <code>useSWR(key, fetcher)</code> returns <code>{ data, error, isLoading, mutate }</code>. Key features: global cache, request deduplication, focus revalidation, interval polling, error retry, and optimistic updates via <code>mutate</code>. SWR is simpler and lighter than React Query but has fewer features (no mutations API, no query cancellation, less control over cache behavior). SWR is popular in Next.js projects (same creator — Vercel).
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q6">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How does React Query's caching work?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q6" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query maintains an in-memory cache keyed by <code>queryKey</code>. When a query is fetched, the result is cached with a <code>staleTime</code> (default: 0 — immediately stale) and <code>gcTime</code> (formerly cacheTime, default: 5 minutes). <code>staleTime</code> controls how long cached data is considered fresh — during this period, components receive cached data without refetching. After staleTime, data is "stale" but still shown while a background refetch occurs. <code>gcTime</code> controls how long unused query data stays in cache before garbage collection. Understanding the distinction: fresh data is never refetched; stale data is shown immediately but refetched in the background; inactive data stays in cache for gcTime then is removed.
      <pre class="code-block">import { useQuery, QueryClient, QueryClientProvider } from '@tanstack/react-query';

const queryClient = new QueryClient({
  defaultOptions: {
    queries: {
      staleTime: 60 * 1000,       // data is fresh for 1 minute — no background refetch
      gcTime: 5 * 60 * 1000,      // cache kept for 5 minutes after component unmounts
      retry: 2,                   // retry failed requests twice
      refetchOnWindowFocus: true, // refetch when user returns to the tab
    },
  },
});

function UserProfile({ userId }) {
  const { data, isLoading, isStale } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['user', userId], // cache key — changing userId triggers new fetch
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
    staleTime: 30 * 1000, // override: fresh for 30 seconds
  });
  return &lt;div&gt;{isStale ? '(refreshing...)' : ''} {data?.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q7">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you invalidate queries in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q7" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>queryClient.invalidateQueries({ queryKey: ['posts'] })</code> marks matching queries as stale and triggers an immediate background refetch if the query is actively subscribed. This is the primary mechanism for keeping server state synchronized after mutations. Invalidation patterns: (1) Exact match: <code>{ queryKey: ['posts', postId], exact: true }</code>; (2) Prefix match: <code>{ queryKey: ['posts'] }</code> matches all queries starting with 'posts' (including ['posts', 1], ['posts', { status: 'published' }]); (3) Invalidate all: <code>queryClient.invalidateQueries()</code>. Best practice: after a mutation that creates/modifies a post, invalidate the posts list so it refetches with the latest data.
      <pre class="code-block">const queryClient = useQueryClient();

const createPost = useMutation({
  mutationFn: (newPost) =&gt; fetch('/api/posts', {
    method: 'POST',
    body: JSON.stringify(newPost)
  }).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
  onSuccess: (createdPost) =&gt; {
    // Invalidate the posts list (triggers background refetch)
    queryClient.invalidateQueries({ queryKey: ['posts'] });

    // Optionally pre-populate the cache for the individual post
    queryClient.setQueryData(['post', createdPost.id], createdPost);
  },
  onError: (error) =&gt; {
    console.error('Failed to create post:', error);
  },
});

function CreatePostForm() {
  const [title, setTitle] = React.useState('');
  return (
    &lt;form onSubmit={(e) =&gt; { e.preventDefault(); createPost.mutate({ title }); }}&gt;
      &lt;input value={title} onChange={(e) =&gt; setTitle(e.target.value)} /&gt;
      &lt;button type="submit" disabled={createPost.isLoading}&gt;Create&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/form&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q8">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is useInfiniteQuery?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q8" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useInfiniteQuery</code> fetches paginated data where each page is determined by a cursor or page number. It accumulates pages in <code>data.pages</code> (an array of page results) and provides <code>fetchNextPage</code>, <code>fetchPreviousPage</code>, <code>hasNextPage</code>, and <code>isFetchingNextPage</code>. The <code>getNextPageParam(lastPage, allPages)</code> function tells React Query how to derive the next page's identifier from the last fetched page. This is the canonical pattern for infinite scroll and load-more pagination.
      <pre class="code-block">function useInfinitePosts() {
  return useInfiniteQuery({
    queryKey: ['posts'],
    queryFn: ({ pageParam = 1 }) =&gt;
      fetch(`/api/posts?page=${pageParam}&amp;limit=10`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
    getNextPageParam: (lastPage, allPages) =&gt;
      lastPage.hasMore ? allPages.length + 1 : undefined,
    initialPageParam: 1,
  });
}

function PostList() {
  const { data, fetchNextPage, hasNextPage, isFetchingNextPage } = useInfinitePosts();
  const sentinelRef = React.useRef(null);

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const observer = new IntersectionObserver(([entry]) =&gt; {
      if (entry.isIntersecting &amp;&amp; hasNextPage) fetchNextPage();
    });
    if (sentinelRef.current) observer.observe(sentinelRef.current);
    return () =&gt; observer.disconnect();
  }, [hasNextPage, fetchNextPage]);

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {data?.pages.flatMap(page =&gt; page.posts).map(post =&gt; (
        &lt;PostCard key={post.id} post={post} /&gt;
      ))}
      &lt;div ref={sentinelRef}&gt;{isFetchingNextPage &amp;&amp; &lt;Spinner /&gt;}&lt;/div&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q9">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement optimistic updates with React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q9" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Optimistic updates in React Query update the cache immediately before the mutation completes, providing instant feedback. If the mutation fails, the previous cache value is restored. The pattern uses <code>onMutate</code> (to save the current cache and apply the optimistic update), <code>onError</code> (to rollback), and <code>onSettled</code> (to invalidate and refetch for the true server state). This gives users instant UI feedback while ensuring eventual consistency with the server.
      <pre class="code-block">const toggleLike = useMutation({
  mutationFn: (postId) =&gt;
    fetch(`/api/posts/${postId}/like`, { method: 'POST' }).then(r =&gt; r.json()),

  onMutate: async (postId) =&gt; {
    // Cancel outgoing refetches to avoid overwriting the optimistic update
    await queryClient.cancelQueries({ queryKey: ['post', postId] });

    // Snapshot the current value for rollback
    const previousPost = queryClient.getQueryData(['post', postId]);

    // Optimistically update the cache
    queryClient.setQueryData(['post', postId], (old) =&gt; ({
      ...old,
      liked: !old.liked,
      likeCount: old.liked ? old.likeCount - 1 : old.likeCount + 1,
    }));

    return { previousPost, postId };
  },

  onError: (err, postId, context) =&gt; {
    // Rollback to the snapshot on error
    queryClient.setQueryData(['post', context.postId], context.previousPost);
  },

  onSettled: (data, error, postId) =&gt; {
    // Always refetch to get the real server state
    queryClient.invalidateQueries({ queryKey: ['post', postId] });
  },
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q10">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is prefetching in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q10" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Prefetching loads query data into the cache before it's needed — so when a component mounts and calls <code>useQuery</code>, the data is already available with no loading state. Use cases: preload the next page of results on hover, preload detail data when hovering a list item, preload data on route transitions. <code>queryClient.prefetchQuery({ queryKey, queryFn })</code> behaves like a query fetch but doesn't trigger a re-render — it just populates the cache silently.
      <pre class="code-block">function usePostListPrefetch() {
  const queryClient = useQueryClient();

  const prefetchPost = React.useCallback((postId) =&gt; {
    queryClient.prefetchQuery({
      queryKey: ['post', postId],
      queryFn: () =&gt; fetch(`/api/posts/${postId}`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
      staleTime: 10 * 1000, // don't prefetch if already fresh
    });
  }, [queryClient]);

  return prefetchPost;
}

function PostListItem({ post }) {
  const prefetchPost = usePostListPrefetch();
  return (
    &lt;div
      onMouseEnter={() =&gt; prefetchPost(post.id)}
      onFocus={() =&gt; prefetchPost(post.id)}
    &gt;
      &lt;a href={`/posts/${post.id}`}&gt;{post.title}&lt;/a&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q11">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is RTK Query and how does it compare to React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q11" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      RTK Query is Redux Toolkit's built-in data fetching solution. It generates hooks (<code>useGetPostQuery</code>, <code>useCreatePostMutation</code>) from an API definition using <code>createApi</code>. Key difference: RTK Query stores fetched data in the Redux store, making it accessible to Redux DevTools and Redux state selectors. React Query stores data in its own cache outside Redux. RTK Query is the right choice when your app already uses Redux and you want a unified state story; React Query is framework-agnostic and simpler to adopt. Both provide: caching, deduplication, invalidation, optimistic updates, and automatic background refetching.
      <pre class="code-block">import { createApi, fetchBaseQuery } from '@reduxjs/toolkit/query/react';

const postsApi = createApi({
  reducerPath: 'postsApi',
  baseQuery: fetchBaseQuery({ baseUrl: '/api/' }),
  tagTypes: ['Post'],
  endpoints: (builder) =&gt; ({
    getPosts: builder.query({
      query: () =&gt; 'posts',
      providesTags: ['Post'],
    }),
    createPost: builder.mutation({
      query: (body) =&gt; ({ url: 'posts', method: 'POST', body }),
      invalidatesTags: ['Post'], // auto-invalidate getPosts after mutation
    }),
  }),
});

export const { useGetPostsQuery, useCreatePostMutation } = postsApi;

function PostList() {
  const { data: posts, isLoading } = useGetPostsQuery();
  const [createPost] = useCreatePostMutation();
  return &lt;div&gt;{posts?.map(p =&gt; &lt;Post key={p.id} post={p} /&gt;)}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q12">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle API errors in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q12" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query retries failed requests automatically (3 times by default with exponential backoff). After retries are exhausted, it sets <code>isError: true</code> and <code>error</code> to the thrown error. Error handling options: (1) per-query <code>onError</code> callback; (2) global <code>QueryClient</code> <code>defaultOptions.queries.onError</code>; (3) React Query 5's <code>throwOnError: true</code> to throw to the nearest Error Boundary; (4) checking <code>isError</code> in the component. For authentication errors (401), use a global error handler to redirect to login.
      <pre class="code-block">const queryClient = new QueryClient({
  defaultOptions: {
    queries: {
      retry: (failureCount, error) =&gt; {
        if (error.status &gt;= 400 &amp;&amp; error.status &lt; 500) return false;
        return failureCount &lt; 3;
      },
    },
  },
  queryCache: new QueryCache({
    onError: (error) =&gt; {
      if (error.status === 401) navigate('/login');
      if (error.status === 403) showToast('Insufficient permissions');
    },
  }),
});

function UserProfile({ userId }) {
  const { data, isLoading, isError, error } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['user', userId],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetchUser(userId),
    throwOnError: false,
  });

  if (isLoading) return &lt;Skeleton /&gt;;
  if (isError) return &lt;ErrorAlert message={error.message} /&gt;;
  return &lt;Profile user={data} /&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q13">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are dependent queries in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q13" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Dependent queries are queries that should only run after another query completes — e.g., fetch user first, then use the user's ID to fetch their projects. The <code>enabled</code> option controls this: <code>enabled: !!userId</code> makes the query wait until userId is truthy. React Query automatically runs the query when <code>enabled</code> changes to true. For parallel independent queries, use <code>useQueries</code> to run multiple queries simultaneously without unnecessary waterfalls.
      <pre class="code-block">function UserProjects({ username }) {
  // Step 1: fetch user by username
  const { data: user } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['user', username],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetchUserByUsername(username),
  });

  // Step 2: only runs when user.id is available
  const { data: projects, isLoading } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['projects', user?.id],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetchProjectsByUser(user.id),
    enabled: !!user?.id,
  });

  if (isLoading) return &lt;Skeleton /&gt;;
  return &lt;ProjectList projects={projects} /&gt;;
}

// Parallel queries — use useQueries
function Dashboard({ userId }) {
  const results = useQueries({
    queries: [
      { queryKey: ['user', userId], queryFn: () =&gt; fetchUser(userId) },
      { queryKey: ['stats', userId], queryFn: () =&gt; fetchStats(userId) },
      { queryKey: ['activity', userId], queryFn: () =&gt; fetchActivity(userId) },
    ],
  });
  const [user, stats, activity] = results;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q14">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle race conditions in data fetching?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q14" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Race conditions occur when multiple fetch requests are in-flight simultaneously and responses arrive out of order — an earlier request's response overwrites the more recent request's result. In React Query, race conditions are handled automatically — it tracks the latest request per query key and ignores responses from superseded requests. For manual <code>useEffect</code> fetching, use either a <code>cancelled</code> flag or <code>AbortController</code>. React Query v5 additionally uses <code>AbortController</code> internally to cancel superseded requests at the network level.
      <pre class="code-block">// Manual fix: cancelled flag
React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
  let cancelled = false;
  setLoading(true);
  fetchData(query)
    .then(data =&gt; { if (!cancelled) setData(data); })
    .finally(() =&gt; { if (!cancelled) setLoading(false); });
  return () =&gt; { cancelled = true; };
}, [query]);

// Manual fix: AbortController
React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
  const controller = new AbortController();
  fetch(`/api/search?q=${query}`, { signal: controller.signal })
    .then(r =&gt; r.json())
    .then(setData)
    .catch(err =&gt; { if (err.name !== 'AbortError') setError(err); });
  return () =&gt; controller.abort();
}, [query]);

// React Query: handled automatically
const { data } = useQuery({
  queryKey: ['search', query],
  queryFn: ({ signal }) =&gt;
    fetch(`/api/search?q=${query}`, { signal }).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q15">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is request deduplication in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q15" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Request deduplication means that if multiple components mount at the same time and call <code>useQuery</code> with the same <code>queryKey</code>, only ONE network request is made — all components receive the same data when it arrives. Without deduplication, 10 components on a page could each trigger the same API request. React Query handles this by checking if a query with that key is already in-flight before starting a new fetch. This dramatically reduces redundant network requests on initial page load. The deduplication window is during the initial fetch — once data is in cache, new subscribers just read from cache instantly.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q16">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement pagination with React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q16" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Standard pagination uses a page number parameter in the query key. Each page is a separate cache entry. <code>placeholderData: keepPreviousData</code> (React Query v5) maintains the previous page's data visible while the next page loads — preventing a loading flash on page change. Prefetching the next page when the current page loads gives users instant navigation.
      <pre class="code-block">import { useQuery, keepPreviousData } from '@tanstack/react-query';

function PostsPage() {
  const [page, setPage] = React.useState(1);

  const { data, isPlaceholderData, isFetching } = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['posts', page],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetch(`/api/posts?page=${page}&amp;limit=10`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
    placeholderData: keepPreviousData,
  });

  const queryClient = useQueryClient();
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    if (!isPlaceholderData &amp;&amp; data?.hasMore) {
      queryClient.prefetchQuery({
        queryKey: ['posts', page + 1],
        queryFn: () =&gt; fetch(`/api/posts?page=${page + 1}`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
      });
    }
  }, [data, page, isPlaceholderData, queryClient]);

  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      {data?.posts.map(post =&gt; &lt;PostCard key={post.id} post={post} /&gt;)}
      &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; setPage(p =&gt; p - 1)} disabled={page === 1}&gt;Prev&lt;/button&gt;
      &lt;span&gt;Page {page}&lt;/span&gt;
      &lt;button onClick={() =&gt; setPage(p =&gt; p + 1)} disabled={!data?.hasMore}&gt;Next&lt;/button&gt;
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q17">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you use AbortController with React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q17" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query v5 automatically passes an <code>AbortSignal</code> to the <code>queryFn</code> via the <code>signal</code> property of the query function context. Pass it to <code>fetch()</code> or axios to automatically cancel in-flight requests when the query is cancelled (component unmounts, query key changes, or query becomes disabled). This saves bandwidth and prevents race conditions at the network level without any manual cleanup code.
      <pre class="code-block">const { data } = useQuery({
  queryKey: ['user', userId],
  queryFn: async ({ signal }) =&gt; {
    const response = await fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`, { signal });
    if (!response.ok) throw new Error(`HTTP ${response.status}`);
    return response.json();
  },
});

// With axios (v0.22+ supports AbortSignal natively)
const queryFn = async ({ signal }) =&gt; {
  const { data } = await axios.get(`/api/users/${userId}`, { signal });
  return data;
};

// Custom fetcher that wraps axios
const axiosFetcher = (url) =&gt; async ({ signal }) =&gt; {
  const { data } = await axios.get(url, { signal });
  return data;
};</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q18">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement WebSocket subscriptions with React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q18" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query is primarily for HTTP request-response patterns. For WebSockets, the pattern is: use <code>useQuery</code> for the initial data load and use <code>queryClient.setQueryData</code> to update the cache when WebSocket messages arrive. This keeps React Query as the single source of truth while WebSocket events trigger cache updates. The component re-renders automatically whenever the cache updates, just as it would after a refetch.
      <pre class="code-block">function useLivePost(postId) {
  const queryClient = useQueryClient();

  // Initial data via HTTP
  const query = useQuery({
    queryKey: ['post', postId],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetch(`/api/posts/${postId}`).then(r =&gt; r.json()),
  });

  // Live updates via WebSocket
  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const ws = new WebSocket(`wss://api.example.com/posts/${postId}`);
    ws.onmessage = (e) =&gt; {
      const update = JSON.parse(e.data);
      // Update the React Query cache directly
      queryClient.setQueryData(['post', postId], (old) =&gt; ({
        ...old,
        ...update,
      }));
    };
    return () =&gt; ws.close();
  }, [postId, queryClient]);

  return query;
}

// Alternative: use queryClient.invalidateQueries on WS message
// triggers refetch instead of manual cache update — simpler but one extra request</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q19">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are Server-Sent Events (SSE) and how do you use them in React?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q19" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      SSE (Server-Sent Events) is a server-push technology where the server streams events to the browser over a persistent HTTP connection. Unlike WebSockets (bidirectional), SSE is unidirectional (server to client). Use cases: live feeds, notifications, progress updates, real-time dashboards. The browser <code>EventSource</code> API manages the connection and automatically reconnects on disconnect. SSE uses standard HTTP, works through firewalls and proxies, and supports the <code>Last-Event-ID</code> header for resuming streams after reconnection.
      <pre class="code-block">function useServerSentEvents(url) {
  const [events, setEvents] = React.useState([]);
  const [status, setStatus] = React.useState('connecting');

  React.useEffect(() =&gt; {
    const eventSource = new EventSource(url);

    eventSource.onopen = () =&gt; setStatus('connected');
    eventSource.onerror = () =&gt; setStatus('error');

    eventSource.onmessage = (e) =&gt; {
      const event = JSON.parse(e.data);
      setEvents(prev =&gt; [...prev, event]);
    };

    eventSource.addEventListener('notification', (e) =&gt; {
      showNotification(JSON.parse(e.data));
    });

    return () =&gt; {
      eventSource.close();
      setStatus('disconnected');
    };
  }, [url]);

  return { events, status };
}

function NotificationFeed({ userId }) {
  const { events, status } = useServerSentEvents(`/api/events?userId=${userId}`);
  return (
    &lt;div&gt;
      &lt;p&gt;Status: {status}&lt;/p&gt;
      {events.map((event, i) =&gt; &lt;NotificationItem key={i} event={event} /&gt;)}
    &lt;/div&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q20">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you handle authentication tokens with React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q20" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Authentication headers (Bearer tokens) should be added to every request via an Axios interceptor or custom fetch wrapper — not inside individual query functions. Token refresh: use an Axios response interceptor that detects 401 responses, refreshes the token silently, and retries the original request. This pattern centralizes auth logic in one place rather than duplicating it across every query.
      <pre class="code-block">const apiClient = axios.create({ baseURL: '/api' });

// Add auth token to every request
apiClient.interceptors.request.use((config) =&gt; {
  const token = authStore.getToken();
  if (token) config.headers.Authorization = `Bearer ${token}`;
  return config;
});

// Token refresh on 401
apiClient.interceptors.response.use(
  (response) =&gt; response,
  async (error) =&gt; {
    if (error.response?.status === 401 &amp;&amp; !error.config._retry) {
      error.config._retry = true;
      const newToken = await authStore.refreshToken();
      error.config.headers.Authorization = `Bearer ${newToken}`;
      return apiClient(error.config); // retry original request
    }
    return Promise.reject(error);
  }
);

// Use the client in React Query
const { data } = useQuery({
  queryKey: ['profile'],
  queryFn: () =&gt; apiClient.get('/profile').then(r =&gt; r.data),
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q21">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the staleTime vs gcTime difference in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q21" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>staleTime</code> (default: 0) determines how long data is considered "fresh" — during this window React Query serves cached data without any background refetch. After staleTime expires, data is "stale" and will be refetched in the background when a component subscribes. <code>gcTime</code> (formerly <code>cacheTime</code>, default: 5 minutes) determines how long query data remains in memory after all subscribed components unmount. During gcTime the data can be served instantly if a component remounts; after gcTime the data is garbage collected and the next mount triggers a fresh fetch with a loading state. Key rule: staleTime should always be less than or equal to gcTime, since there is no point keeping data in cache longer than it can be considered usable.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q22">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you implement a global loading indicator with React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q22" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>useIsFetching()</code> returns the count of currently fetching queries across the entire app. Use it to drive a global loading bar whenever any query is fetching. This is perfect for slim top-bar progress indicators that give subtle feedback without blocking the UI. Place the indicator component at the app root outside the router so it is always visible regardless of route.
      <pre class="code-block">import { useIsFetching } from '@tanstack/react-query';

function GlobalFetchingIndicator() {
  const isFetching = useIsFetching();
  return isFetching &gt; 0
    ? &lt;div className="global-loading-bar" role="progressbar" aria-label="Loading..." /&gt;
    : null;
}

function App() {
  return (
    &lt;QueryClientProvider client={queryClient}&gt;
      &lt;GlobalFetchingIndicator /&gt;
      &lt;Router&gt;{/* routes */}&lt;/Router&gt;
    &lt;/QueryClientProvider&gt;
  );
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q23">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you mock API calls for React Query in tests?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q23" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The recommended approach is MSW (Mock Service Worker) — it intercepts real network requests so your actual fetch/axios code runs unchanged. Wrap the component in a <code>QueryClientProvider</code> with a fresh <code>QueryClient</code> per test and retries disabled to prevent slow tests on expected errors.
      <pre class="code-block">import { render, screen, waitFor } from '@testing-library/react';
import { QueryClient, QueryClientProvider } from '@tanstack/react-query';
import { rest } from 'msw';
import { setupServer } from 'msw/node';

const server = setupServer(
  rest.get('/api/users/:id', (req, res, ctx) =&gt;
    res(ctx.json({ id: '1', name: 'Alice' }))
  )
);
beforeAll(() =&gt; server.listen());
afterEach(() =&gt; server.resetHandlers());
afterAll(() =&gt; server.close());

function createWrapper() {
  const qc = new QueryClient({ defaultOptions: { queries: { retry: false } } });
  return ({ children }) =&gt; (
    &lt;QueryClientProvider client={qc}&gt;{children}&lt;/QueryClientProvider&gt;
  );
}

test('displays user name', async () =&gt; {
  render(&lt;UserProfile userId="1" /&gt;, { wrapper: createWrapper() });
  expect(screen.getByText('Loading...')).toBeInTheDocument();
  await waitFor(() =&gt; expect(screen.getByText('Alice')).toBeInTheDocument());
});</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q24">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What are query key best practices in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q24" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Query keys serve as cache keys serialized for uniqueness. Best practices: (1) Always use arrays — <code>['user', userId]</code> is composable; (2) Include every variable used in queryFn so cache auto-invalidates when inputs change; (3) Use a query key factory to centralize definitions and prevent typos; (4) Order from general to specific so prefix invalidation works — <code>['posts']</code> matches <code>['posts', 1]</code> and <code>['posts', { status: 'published' }]</code>; (5) Include user identity in user-scoped queries to prevent data leakage between accounts on shared devices.
      <pre class="code-block">const queryKeys = {
  all: ['posts'] as const,
  lists: () =&gt; [...queryKeys.all, 'list'] as const,
  list: (filters: PostFilters) =&gt; [...queryKeys.lists(), filters] as const,
  details: () =&gt; [...queryKeys.all, 'detail'] as const,
  detail: (id: number) =&gt; [...queryKeys.details(), id] as const,
};

useQuery({ queryKey: queryKeys.list({ status: 'published' }), queryFn: ... });
useQuery({ queryKey: queryKeys.detail(postId), queryFn: ... });

// Invalidate all lists
queryClient.invalidateQueries({ queryKey: queryKeys.lists() });
// Invalidate one detail
queryClient.invalidateQueries({ queryKey: queryKeys.detail(postId) });</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q25">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the difference between isFetching and isLoading in React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q25" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      <code>isLoading</code> (renamed <code>isPending</code> in React Query v5) is <code>true</code> only on the very first fetch when there is no cached data — it signals "nothing to show yet, render a skeleton." <code>isFetching</code> is <code>true</code> whenever a request is in-flight, including background refetches where stale data is already visible. Use <code>isLoading</code> to gate the initial skeleton. Use <code>isFetching &amp;&amp; !isLoading</code> (equivalently <code>isRefetching</code>) to show a subtle corner spinner alongside existing content during a background refresh. Combining them correctly avoids both the "blank flash" problem and the "stale data without any indicator" problem.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q26">
      <span class="diff-medium">Medium</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How do you use React Query with TypeScript?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q26" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query is fully typed via generics. Provide <code>useQuery&lt;TData, TError&gt;</code> so <code>data</code> is inferred as <code>TData | undefined</code> and <code>error</code> as <code>TError | null</code>. The best pattern is wrapping queries in typed custom hooks so callers get full IntelliSense without repeating generic annotations.
      <pre class="code-block">interface User { id: number; name: string; email: string; }
interface ApiError { message: string; status: number; }

function useUser(userId: number) {
  return useQuery&lt;User, ApiError&gt;({
    queryKey: ['user', userId],
    queryFn: async (): Promise&lt;User&gt; =&gt; {
      const res = await fetch(`/api/users/${userId}`);
      if (!res.ok) throw { message: 'Not found', status: res.status } as ApiError;
      return res.json();
    },
  });
}

function UserCard({ userId }: { userId: number }) {
  const { data: user, isLoading, error } = useUser(userId);
  if (isLoading) return &lt;Skeleton /&gt;;
  if (error) return &lt;p&gt;{error.message}&lt;/p&gt;;
  return &lt;div&gt;{user?.name}&lt;/div&gt;;
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q27">
      <span class="diff-hard">Hard</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;What is the select option in useQuery?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q27" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      The <code>select</code> option transforms cached data at read time before it reaches the component's <code>data</code> property. The full queryFn result is stored in cache; <code>select</code> applies a transformation on every render. Multiple components can read the same cache key but each receive a different slice. Crucially, <code>select</code> participates in structural sharing — if the transformed result is referentially equal to the previous one the component will not re-render, even if unrelated parts of the cached response changed.
      <pre class="code-block">// Component only receives the name string, not the full User object
function useUserName(userId: number) {
  return useQuery({
    queryKey: ['user', userId],
    queryFn: () =&gt; fetchUser(userId),
    select: (user) =&gt; user.name,
  });
}

function UserNameDisplay({ userId }) {
  const name = useUserName(userId);  // data: string | undefined
  return &lt;span&gt;{name}&lt;/span&gt;;
}

// Extract a single post from a cached list query
function usePost(postId: number) {
  return useQuery({
    queryKey: ['posts'],
    queryFn: fetchAllPosts,
    select: (posts) =&gt; posts.find(p =&gt; p.id === postId),
  });
}</pre>
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q28">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;Your app fetches the same user data in 10 different components. How does React Query handle this efficiently?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q28" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      React Query handles this through automatic request deduplication and a shared in-memory cache. When 10 components mount simultaneously and all call <code>useQuery({ queryKey: ['user', userId] })</code>, React Query detects the key is already in-flight and makes exactly ONE network request. All 10 components receive data when that single request resolves — zero configuration required. After data is cached, subsequent mounts receive it instantly from cache until the data becomes stale. When a background refetch is triggered, again only ONE refetch is made regardless of subscriber count. Without React Query, preventing duplicate requests would require manual coordination via a global flag, shared context, or Redux — React Query provides this behavior as a built-in core principle.
    </div>
  </div>
</div>

<div class="accordion-item">
  <h2 class="accordion-header">
    <button class="accordion-button collapsed" type="button" data-bs-toggle="collapse" data-bs-target="#api-q29">
      <span class="diff-scenario">Scenario</span>&nbsp;&nbsp;How would you design a real-time notification system using SSE and React Query?
    </button>
  </h2>
  <div id="api-q29" class="accordion-collapse collapse" data-bs-parent="#acc-api">
    <div class="accordion-body">
      Combine an initial HTTP fetch for existing notifications with an SSE stream for live updates. React Query owns the cache; SSE events write into it via <code>queryClient.setQueryData</code> so all subscribed components re-render automatically. The browser EventSource reconnects automatically on connection drop.
      <pre class="code-block">function useNotifications(userId: string) {
  const queryClient = useQueryClient();
